============================================================
  01-Course_Introduction
============================================================

Let's start the course by understanding the DevOps prerequisites of a software provider.
Over the duration of this course, we will check into and learn about how GitLab CI/CD
can be used to meet these DevOps requirements.
Dasher Technology is a software provider which offers a platform to facilitate the connection
of data, applications, and devices across on-premises environments for businesses.
Recently, their research and development team has been exploring the possibilities of transitioning
their services to the cloud and leveraging container technologies. Initially, they aimed
to apply this to one of their NodeJS-based projects with intentions to extend this
approach to other projects developed in Java and Python at a later stage.
They introduced a DevOps team for this initiative, which is led by Alice, who will be responsible
for establishing the DevOps pipeline for the project right from its inception,
adhering to industry best practices. Their work will revolve around a multi-cloud infrastructure
utilizing Docker for containerization and Kubernetes for container orchestration.
Alice conducted a swift evaluation and learned that the current requirement pertains to a
NodeJS-based project. The previous team operated without a version control system,
which developers independently wrote and manually integrated code. The testing process was sluggish
and ineffective due to manual execution. Collaboration among developers was often
hampered as they worked on separate code branches. With infrequent integrations and testing,
software releases carried more significant risks. Deployment of software to various environments,
including development, staging, and production, was primarily a manual procedure. To tackle
these challenges, Alice and her team have opted to implement a continuous integration
and a continuous deployment pipeline and have outlined the following key steps.
First, they would like to adopt GitLab for version control and developer collaboration,
and implementation of unit testing and code coverage measures to expedite testing and
minimizing bugs, utilization of Docker builds and push processes for containerization,
and the application is then deployed to Kubernetes clusters.
The pipeline should also be incorporated with various automated integration testing as a final
step. The successful execution of these steps is expected to resolve the existing issues.
Nevertheless, the team now faces the additional hurdles of selecting the most suitable CI/CD tool.
In the market, a wide array of CI/CD tools are available, including Jenkins, Travis CI, Bamboo,
Spinnaker, GitHub Actions, CircleCI, and more. After exploring several options, the team has
made the decision to proceed with Jenkins as it stands out as one of the most extensively used
open-source CI/CD tools. To employ Jenkins, the team is required to undertake several tasks.
One is to configuration and operation and upkeeping of virtual machines with specific CPU,
memory, and hard disk capacity, and prior to Jenkins installation, prerequisites include
Java JDK installation, setting up firewall rules, and installing various different Jenkins plugins.
And the last one, given this project is centered around NodeJS,
NodeJS and NPM must also be installed.
So, to facilitate unit testing with various NodeJS versions, the DevOps engineers must install
multiple NodeJS NPM versions, ensuring that the test runs without any version conflicts,
and Docker installation is required for doing the containerization steps.
Apart from that, for Kubernetes deployment, the installation of kubectl, Helm, or other
necessary binaries is necessary. Teams often employ external tools for integration testing
and reporting, which also requires the installation of these tools or their command-line interfaces
on the machine where Jenkins will be executed. It is important to note that these tasks apply
to a single NodeJS-based project with high-level steps. The pipeline's complexity may grow with
additional steps proportionally increasing the manual configuration workload. Furthermore,
the organization intends to implement similar methodologies for other Java and Python projects
running on cloud platforms like AWS and Azure. Depending on the specific project,
additional software requirements such as Java, Maven, Python, Azure, AWS CLIs may be necessary.
And if the pipeline demands enhanced security in line with DevSecOps practices,
more tools like Privy and KubeSec must also be configured on the same Jenkins instance.
Considering that this DevOps team is recently established, many team members lack familiarity
with a broad spectrum of tools and services. Alice has initiated a search for a tool that
satisfies the following criteria: simplified setup and initiation without the need to install
and configure numerous services and a focus on building pipelines without the burden of
managing infrastructure or concerns regarding scalability. Following a thorough evaluation of
multiple tools, Alice has chosen to check and implement GitLab CI/CD. Over the course of this
training program, we will check into the creation of GitLab CI/CD pipelines for a straightforward
yet real-time NodeJS application. Thank you.

============================================================
  02-Problem_Statement_(Meeting_with_XYZ_Team)
============================================================

Let's go ahead and explore the basics of CI/CD.
In a typical project, source code resides in a Git repository where it's both stored
and versioned. GitLab is a web-based platform centered around Git, serves as a centralized
hub for hosting these repositories and extends Git's capabilities with additional features.
Typically, all code resides on the main or master branch, which is frequently deployed
to production servers or environments. When there is a need to introduce new features
or simply modify existing code, developers create and collaborate on what's known as
a feature branch. This branch essentially functions as a clone of the main codebase,
enabling a team of developers to work on a new feature until it's fully developed.
Once the necessary changes are made, the code is committed to the feature branch,
and a Merge Request is initiated to merge this code back into the main branch.
But before the merge takes place, a review process is carried out and the changes require
approval from relevant team members or individuals. Following a successful merge
into the main branch, the code is then deployed either manually or through an automated process
to the production environment. This situation poses a significant risk to the application's
stability as there is often no testing conducted on the newly merged code before it reaches the
production environment. In real-time scenarios, you will often find multiple developers working
on different feature branches, each focusing on a new enhancement. Performing multiple merges
without the implementation of continuous integration in a software development workflow
can lead to several significant problems and challenges such as delayed testing,
inefficient deployment, and quality assurance challenges. Talking about delayed testing,
without CI, testing typically occurs late in the development cycle, often after multiple
merges have taken place. This delay in testing can make it harder to identify and rectify issues
early in the development process, which increases the risk of defects making their way into production.
Talking about inefficient deployment, in the absence of CI, deploying code to various
environments like dev, stage, production often relies on manual processes. This can lead to
inconsistencies in deployment and potential configuration errors. And finally, without
automated testing as an integral part of the development process, ensuring software quality
becomes more reliant on manual testing, making it prone to human errors and subject to resource
constraints. So now let's imagine a scenario where a developer 1 creates a feature branch A,
makes some modifications, and commits the code to this branch. A pull request is generated
to merge these changes into the main branch. But before the merge, a team member reviews
the code and an automated CI pipeline is triggered. The CI pipeline proceeds through
several stages including unit testing, dependency scanning, artifact building, and vulnerability
code scanning. All these assessments are performed on both the newly added code and the existing code
from the main branch. If any of these tests fail, the developer is asked to make the necessary
adjustments and commit the changes to the same pull request. This action triggers the CI pipeline
again and if there are no failure symptoms, the pull request is approved and merged into the
main branch. Upon merging into the main branch, the same CI pipeline or possibly a different one
with additional steps and tests is automatically executed to verify the merged code. At this point,
testing the same code again after merging may seem redundant, but I'll try to clarify this
by the end of this video. So while these developments are taking place in feature branch A,
developer 2 is progressing on feature branch B. They commit the changes and create a pull request
and whenever the pull request is created, an automated CI pipeline is initiated,
testing the code committed in this new feature branch B. So once the CI pipeline successfully
completes, the pull request is approved and it can also be merged back to the main branch.
So following the successful merge, the main branch now contains the code changes from both feature
branch A and B. As mentioned earlier, any merges into the main branch should automatically trigger
a CI/CD pipeline. In this instance, it once again runs all the tests including the unit tests,
dependency scanning, artifact building, and additional vulnerability scans. This ensures
that the code changes from both feature branch A and B work seamlessly together. This entire process
which enables multiple developers to work on the same application while ensuring that these changes
integrate smoothly without introducing any issues is known as continuous integration.
So far, we explored the concept of continuous integration. Now let's check into the CD part
which could be continuous delivery or continuous deployment. So in the previous instance,
after a code integration is done into the main branch and successful completion of the CI
pipeline, manual deployment to the production environment was performed. In many scenarios,
even after rigorous CI and testing procedures, it is advisable to deploy the modified application
to a non-production environment that closely resembles the live environment.
This allows for live testing before proceeding with production deployment. So within the same
feature branch, following a successful continuous integration pipeline run, we can establish
another continuous deployment pipeline. This pipeline should be responsible for deploying
the modified code to either a staging or a development environment. So following deployment,
a series of tests are executed to ensure that the quality of the application does not get degraded.
Upon successful completion of CD, the pull request is approved and merged back into the main branch.
So within the main branch, the CI/CD pipeline is triggered assessing the newly merged changes.
If successful, it automatically initiates the CD pipeline which results in the deployment of the
application to the production environment. This automatic deployment process following successful
continuous integration is often referred to as continuous deployment. In certain instances,
a manual approval step before production deployment is a critical aspect of the
deployment process. This step serves to minimize risks, ensure quality,
adhere to compliance requirements, and effectively coordinate changes. It offers a safety net and
allows for human judgment and oversight within an otherwise automated process. So in this scenario,
following the successful completion of the CI pipeline, the CD pipeline awaits human approval
before proceeding with the production deployment. This process of manual approval
prior to production deployment is known as continuous delivery. Thank you.

============================================================
  03-Basics_of_CICD
============================================================

Let's assume an organization has decided to use GitLab as their core repository
and is in search of a CI/CD automation solution.
GitLab Server is an open-source DevOps platform that allows you to manage your entire software
development lifecycle from a single location. It provides features for version control,
issue tracking, CI/CD pipelines, package registries, and many more.
There are numerous tools available in the market to automate CI/CD pipelines.
Nevertheless, given the organization's commitment to GitLab as the core repository or the version
control system, let's explore how GitLab CI/CD offers the automation capabilities.
So what is GitLab CI/CD?
GitLab CI/CD is a built-in tool that helps you automate your software development lifecycle
from code commit to deployment. It allows developers to automate tasks directly from
their repositories. You can quickly create workflows or pipelines to implement a CI/CD
job that builds, tests on every commit, and also deploys merge requests to any environment
right beside your codebase.
So how are these CI/CD jobs executed?
Runners are the agents that run your jobs.
GitLab Runner works with GitLab CI/CD to run jobs in a pipeline.
Out of the box, GitLab CI/CD offers two main options – software as a service (SaaS) runners
and self-managed runners. In this session, we'll have a brief look at the SaaS runners
and later on, we'll also explore self-managed runners in a separate segment.
SaaS Runners are hosted and managed by GitLab.
These runners fully integrate with GitLab.com server and are enabled by default for all
projects with no configuration required.
Your jobs can run on Linux Runners, which support a wide range of languages and tools.
You can also choose Windows Runners, which are ideal for projects that use Windows-specific
tools or require testing on Windows systems.
You can also make use of macOS Runners, which is a good choice for projects that
require macOS-specific tools and services.
And finally, it also provides you GPU-enabled SAST Runners to accelerate heavy computing
workloads for high-performance computing, such as the training or deployment of large
language models as part of ModelOps workloads.
Another advantage of SaaS Runners is that GitLab manages the infrastructure for you,
which includes setting up servers, scaling resources,
and also managing the execution environment for your workflows.
So, your task is to write the workflow configuration, which is basically in YAML
and GitLab CI/CD handles the rest of the stuff.
This includes executing your tasks within a newly provisioned virtual machine for each job,
caching necessary dependencies, and also providing reports on the outcomes.
Finally, the automation helps to streamline development by releasing new features
and bug fixes to your users more quickly.
It can also automate your deployment so you can reduce manual errors.
And finally, it can increase the efficiency of your software by catching bugs early and often
before they make it to the production environment.
So, how does this work?
So, let's just check out the YAML file, which is used for creating and running these pipeline jobs.
So, to kickstart, you will be starting with a .gitlab-ci.yaml file in your project's root
directory, which contains the configuration for your CI/CD pipeline.
And a pipeline is nothing but an automated process capable of executing one or more jobs.
These pipeline jobs, in response, I mean,
this is basically run in response to specific events occurring in your repository.
For instance, in this example, a simple event
like committing code to the main branch of the repository is going to trigger the pipeline.
And within the pipeline, you can define one or more jobs.
Jobs define what you want to do.
For example, you want to test whatever code changes that you're committing,
or you want to deploy, you know, the code changes to a different environment.
And a runner is a virtual machine that is responsible for running your pipeline
jobs upon triggering. GitLab, as we discussed,
automatically provisions SAST runners for each job based on the tag specified.
In this example, there is a job named as unit test job that runs on three different machines,
Windows, Ubuntu, and macOS. And consequently, GitLab will provision
these three virtual machines for these jobs. And these virtual machines are known as, you know,
GitLab hosted SAST runners. In this particular example,
all the runners are provisioned and operated in parallel.
And you can also change the behavior if required. So once the runners spin up, by default,
GitLab Runner performs a clone of the repository to fetch the latest commits.
After cloning, the runners start the execution of the steps or commands,
you know, which are defined within the script of the YAML file.
So in this example, or in this example workflow,
there are two commands and they are executed sequentially.
First, it's going to run the npm install command,
and it's going to install the NodeJS packages on the runner machines.
And then it's going to run the npm test command to execute the defined unit test cases.
These same steps are executed on all the three runners concurrently.
And consequently, if one of the runners completes all the steps execution,
it will be marked as successful, even if the other runners are still in progress of,
you know, executing their remaining steps.
The pipeline as a whole is marked as successful
only once all the three runners have completed the task successfully.
So during and after the job execution, if you want to access the logs,
the outputs or any artifacts for each job,
you can access them via the GitLab user interface, or you can also use the GitLab REST API.
So this information is basically received by GitLab from the runners
where the jobs get executed.
So within the GitLab repository, you can simply navigate to the Pipeline tab
to inspect the Pipeline status, the logs and the artifacts.
And once you go to the stage, you will be finding that there is a job named as unit testing.
It has been executed on three different machines,
and you can view the logs of each of them individually.
This is going to allow you for detailed debugging of the workflow's progress and its results.
And if the job generates any artifacts, you can access and download them as well.
So GitLab provides a very convenient interface for managing and retrieving any artifacts
produced during this workflow execution,
and therefore enhancing the overall visibility and usability of your automated processes.
So finally, in this video, I provided a brief overview of pipelines, jobs, scripts, and runners.
So these concepts will be explored in greater detail in our upcoming sessions.
Thank you.

gives you a list of all the projects which you have in your account.
So these are like basically, it creates a namespace. If you are creating an account for
the first time, you have two types of namespaces, which are used for organizing your projects.
So one is a personal namespace, which gets created using your username. And it contains
all the projects which are created in this namespace. And the other one is groups.
So in GitLab, you use groups to manage one or more related projects at the same time.

So in GitLab, you can create projects to host your codebase. You can also use projects to track

account has access of up to 400 compute units every month. So what are these? So we will be
making use of GitLab CI/CD, and we will be running the pipelines using the GitLab Shared Virtual
Machines. So the virtual machines are not free. So it's going to calculate the time taken for running
the jobs, and those are known as compute units. So compute is the resource consumed when you're
running a pipeline on the GitLab Shared Virtual Machine. And as I've told you, so I can use around
400 units, and it has various different ways of calculating the units, and also the pricing as
well. So if you want to, you know, look at the pricing model, you can simply click on this icon,
it takes you to a different page, and it describes everything about what is the compute quota,
how you can set up these quotas for different namespaces, groups, projects, and it also shows
you like how this usage is calculated. So the formula which is used is, you know, it's going
to calculate the usage based on the duration of the job multiplied by the cost factor.
So the cost factor, again, depends upon a couple of things. So one is the project visibility.
So it depends upon if your project is an internal project, public or a private project, and there
is a different cost factor if you're working on some, you know, open source programs. And apart
from that, the cost also depends upon the type of the machine which you're using for running
your pipelines. So by default, GitLab supports Linux, Windows, macOS, and also GPU-enabled,
you know, virtual machines or runners. And based on the machine, and the machine size,
the cost factor differs for different machines and the, you know, the sizes, right. So we'll
be exploring more about the GitLab CI/CD, its, you know, components, features, what are these
runners, the types of runners, how to set up the pipelines, and much more in this upcoming
session. Thank you.

============================================================
  05-Create_GitLab_Account
============================================================

In this session, we will explore the core concepts of a GitLab CI/CD pipeline.
It consists of four key components: pipeline, stages, jobs, and script.
So let's take an example to understand them.
A pipeline is an automated process capable of executing one or more jobs.
It's going to act as the blueprint for continuous integration and deployment.
It's going to ensure that the code changes flow smoothly through testing,
building, and the deployment phases.
The pipeline is defined using a YAML file,
and it is located within your repository right beside your codebase.
To uniquely identify different pipelines,
each pipeline can have an optional workflow named keyword.
So this name will be visible in the Pipelines tab of the GitLab repository.
Pipelines are triggered by events such as code pushes, Merge Requests, or manual actions.
In this example, the rules keyword within the workflow is going to trigger the pipeline
when there is a commit to the main branch.
We will talk more about triggers and rules in a later session.
The next core component is a job.
Jobs are the building blocks of the pipelines,
and you can have one or more jobs within the same pipeline.
Each job is associated with a runner.
It could be a GitLab-hosted or a self-hosted runner.
These runners are basically virtual machines,
which are responsible for running the job's instructions.
We can optionally define a tags keyword to specify the runner.
And in this example, the job will run on a Linux machine.
And if there is no tag keyword within your job,
the jobs will run on a default Runner.
So we will talk more about tags, runners, and the runner configurations in a later session.
So the core component of a job is called a script.
So the script contains the commands which need to be executed.
It is like the heart of the pipeline, defining the specific tasks which have to be performed.
So scripts can be written as individual commands,
as a shell script, or other supported languages.
It's going to offer flexibility in different automation tools.
So all the steps or commands within a script are executed sequentially.
And typically, scripts include commands which are used for building code,
running tests, deploying artifacts, performing security scans,
and also in some cases, generating documentation.
And each job can also have a before and or after scripts.
So these are optional scripts that run before or after the main script of a job,
providing opportunities for setup and teardown activities.
So the common use cases of the before script is to install the dependencies,
preparing test environment, or setting up some database connections.
And some common use cases of the after script include
cleaning up the resources after a pipeline execution is completed.
And if a pipeline has multiple jobs, by default,
all the jobs are going to be executed in parallel at the same time.
So to logically group jobs within a pipeline, we use a concept of stages.
So stages provide a logical grouping of jobs within a pipeline.
They represent distinct phases of the CI/CD process,
often aligned with the development milestones.
So stages execute jobs in a predetermined order, thereby enabling conditional execution,
allowing flexibility based on outcomes of the previous stages,
optimizing resource usage, and also preventing unnecessary actions.
So the common stages include building such as compiling the code,
you know, testing, running automated tests,
deploying the code from one environment to another environment.
And finally, you know, scanning for vulnerabilities using the security stage.
In this example, we have defined two stages, test and deploy.
And hence, the deploy job will start execution
only after the unit testing job successfully completes its execution.
And talking about pipelines.
In GitLab CI/CD, they are, I mean, it offers a wide range of pipelines.
And it has, you know, different configurations, which can be,
you know, used to tailor your software delivery process.
So we have the basic pipeline, which executes the jobs within a stage concurrently,
moving to the next stage only after all the jobs in the current stage are completed.
We have DAG pipelines, which can optimize efficiency by running jobs based on dependencies,
potentially reducing execution time compared to basic pipelines.
We have Merge Request pipelines, which trigger specifically for Merge Request,
ensuring Code Quality and consistency before merging branches.
We have something similar called as merge request pipeline,
which simulates the merge state of a merge request,
providing early insights into potential conflicts or issues.
Then we have merge train pipelines, which enact the Merge Request for sequential execution,
ensuring a controlled and orderly integration process.
We have parent-child pipelines, which are used for managing the complexity
by breaking down extensive pipelines into a parent pipeline that orchestrates multiple
child pipelines often used in a monorepo environment.
And finally, we have the multi-project pipelines,
which can coordinate pipelines across different projects,
fostering collaboration and streamlining cross-project dependencies.
So we can choose the pipeline, you know, that best suits your workflow,
team structure and project needs to achieve efficient and reliable software delivery.
Thank you.

the runner. So if you don't define any runner, by default, it's going to make use of the GitLab
shared runners. And it's going to use one of the available runners. And if you don't define a runner,
it's going to by default use a runner of size small. And within this runner, or within this
virtual machine, it also requires an executor in order to, you know, execute or run your script
commands. So the first step is it's going to prepare the executor. And as you can see here,
it is using a Docker machine executor. And in this executor, it's making use of a Ruby
container image. So it's going to create a container based on this image. And within that
container, it's going to, you know, execute your job. So if you see the next one is about preparing
the environment. So it's running your job within this executor. So first, it's going to get the
source code from your Git repository. It's basically like, you know, checking out your
code repository to this specific runner. And you can see that it is, you know, just pulling the
latest commit, right. So this is the commit ID, which you can see on the right side as well.
Right. So once the commit or the repository codebase is, you know, checked out, the next step
is executing the step script. So whatever commands you define in the script phase will be executed
here. And again, it's saying that all those commands are executed within the same container,
which was created earlier. So here, it's going to show you the command and the output of the
command as well. So first, we used echo command, it's going to show you the echo,
then we used the list command. So when we committed this, it only had one README.md file,
but it cannot show you the GitLab CI YAML file because that is a hidden file, right. So that's
the reason it's not visible over here. And then I used cat README.md. So it's just going to print
out the entire README.md content. And finally, after it completes all the, you know, scripts
and the commands, if there are no errors, it's simply just going to clean up the project directory
and the job gets succeeded, right. So that is how you can create a basic pipeline and run it
without any additional configuration, right. So again, the same details can be seen on the
right side, like the name of the runner or the type of the runner which is used,
it gives you the pipeline's unique ID, it shows you the branch details, it shows you the duration,
like how much time it took to complete this, you know, pipeline execution. So that's all for now.
So in the next sessions, we'll talk more about the core components like
the runners, the executors and the types of runners. Thank you.

============================================================
  07-Create_and_Run_a_Basic_Pipeline
============================================================

Building on our previous demo of pipeline definition and execution,
we will now focus on the key components of GitLab CI/CD.
So, the three components of the GitLab CI/CD are GitLab Server, Runner, and Executor.
In this session, we'll discuss the Runner.
So, these Runners are external machines or virtual machines responsible for executing pipeline jobs.
They register with the GitLab Server to receive and process job instructions.
You have two main options, Shared Runners and the Self-Managed Runners.
Each has its own set of advantages and disadvantages,
so choosing the right one for your needs is important.
So, talking about the Shared Runners, so these are the Runners which are provided
and managed by GitLab. They are available to all projects on a GitLab
SaaS instance and are used on a first-come, first-served basis.
So, talking about the pros, so the Shared Runners are, you know, easy to use because
you don't need to set up or, you know, do any kind of maintenance, you just start using them.
And it's also cost-effective because it's included in your GitLab subscription and some of your
tiers. And finally, it's also scalable as GitLab automatically scales the number of Runners to meet
the demands. Whereas, the Self-Managed Runners are the Runners that you install and manage yourself
on your own infrastructure. And even this has some, you know, advantages like, you know,
you have complete control over the Runner configuration, environment, and security.
And performance-wise, these Runners can be faster and more reliable than the Shared Runners.
And since these Runners are isolated from other projects, potentially, it's going to reduce the
security risks. And we also need to know the challenges. So, challenges of the Shared Runner
include, you know, limited control where you don't have the control over the Runner's configuration
or the environment where it runs. The jobs may also, you know, be slower due to the competition
for resources. And finally, you know, the Runner shares resources with other projects,
potentially, you know, increasing security risks. And talking about the Self-Managed Runner,
it is complex to set up and maintain as this requires technical expertise to install,
configure, and maintain the Runners, you know, in addition to, you know, creating the pipelines.
And, you know, this can be difficult to scale as your needs grow. And finally, you know,
you may require additional hardware and software costs to, you know, just keep them up and running.
Thank you.

============================================================
  08-Types_of_Runners
============================================================

In the last session, we talked about GitLab CI/CD runners, which are essentially workers which
execute your pipeline jobs. So what are executors? Why do we need them? So runners themselves don't
directly run the jobs. They utilize something called an executor to actually carry out
the execution based on the specific needs of your project. And under the hood of each runner
lies a hidden engine called an executor. So what are these GitLab runner executors?
So you can think of an executor as a container within your runner. You can define the operating
system, tools, and the resources a job has to access. And they ensure that the jobs run in a
consistent and isolated environment. It's going to prevent conflicts and any unexpected behavior.
So GitLab offers a wide variety of built-in executors, each with its own pros and cons.
So choosing the right executor is crucial for ensuring efficient, secure, and reliable pipeline
execution. There are around 10 different types of GitLab Runner executors. So let's have a look at
some of the most widely used executors. So let's start with the Shell Executor. So this is a very
basic executor, which runs jobs directly on the runner's operating system using its shell like
Bash or PowerShell. It is very simple and lightweight. It's also suitable for quick
scripts or for testing basic functionality. And it has some disadvantages like it's going to share
the runner's operating system and resources. So it's going to always have a risk of conflicting
and unexpected behavior. And the results may also vary depending on the runner's environment
and the installed tools. And finally, sharing the resources might pose security risks if the
job requires different levels of access. The next executor we'll discuss is the
Docker Executor. So for greater isolation and reproducibility, Docker executors run jobs
inside a Docker container. These containers encapsulate the specific environments like the
operating system, libraries, tools needed for the job, guaranteeing consistency across multiple
machines. We can also choose from the countless pre-built Docker images with specific tools and
environments which are readily available. And of course, disadvantages are it requires
additional configuration and understanding of Docker concepts. You also need to run multiple
containers and it can consume more system resources on the runner. And finally, the
Docker containers often have restricted access to the runner's native resources.
And the third type of executor is a Kubernetes executor. If your project utilizes a Kubernetes
cluster, the Kubernetes executor lets you seamlessly run jobs as parts within those
clusters. This is going to leverage the cluster's resource management and scaling capabilities
for efficient execution and parallel processing. And of course, talking about the disadvantages,
it's going to have a very complex setup and requires complex maintenance because you need
to have familiarity with Kubernetes configuration and management in order to manage the executor
within a pod. And then it may not be feasible if you don't have a Kubernetes cluster. So,
there is a potential dependency on existing infrastructure. And finally, it's going to
have an increased learning curve because you need to first understand the best practices
and security considerations for running the jobs within Kubernetes.
The fourth type of executor is a virtual machine. So, if you need a completely clean slate for each
job, the VM executor is going to create a new virtual machine for each job execution,
guaranteeing isolation and eliminating potential contamination from previous ones. And of course,
it has its own disadvantages. One is it's going to have a very high resource usage because VMs
require significant disk space, memory, and CPU resources, potentially increasing your
infrastructure cost. And it's also going to have slower startup times because creating
and initializing VMs takes longer than starting containers, slowing down pipeline executions.
And finally, it also requires expertise in VM management and configuration.
The fifth type of executor which we will discuss is the SSH executor. This executor is going to
allow running jobs remotely on another machine via SSH connections. It is going to be useful
for utilizing specific resources available on a different machine or integrating with pre-existing
infrastructure. Talking about the challenges or disadvantages, it always has security concerns
because it requires secure SSH access and careful configurations to maintain jobs and system
security. It also relies on reliable network connectivity between the runner and the remote
machine. And finally, it has limited control and visibility because managing and monitoring jobs
running on a remote machine might be more challenging. And finally, the last type of
executor which we will discuss today is the custom executor. So GitLab offers additional
specialized executors like Parallel, Docker Machine, and even some custom ones which you can
develop yourself. So each is going to cater to a specific need and environments, ensuring flexibility
and catering to the diverse workflows. And talking about the disadvantages, it's also going
to have overhead and complexity because you have to create your own executor and start using it.
So you need to know how to develop it. And it's going to also increase the resource usage.
And the third one is it's going to have limited access to the underlying systems.
So these executors support different platforms and methodologies for building a project.
So this is an official table which shows the key facts for each executor, which will help you to
decide which one to use. And I would also recommend you to check out the official documentation
for more information. Thank you.

============================================================
  09-Types_of_Executors
============================================================

Building on our previous sessions of Runners and Executors, we will now focus on how these
components work together and orchestrate the entire process. So far we discussed about the
Runner and Executor, but what is this GitLab Server? GitLab Server is an open-source DevOps
platform that allows you to manage your entire software development lifecycle from a single
location. It provides features like version control, issue tracking, CI/CD pipelines,
package registries, and much more. So it comes in two flavors, GitLab SaaS and the GitLab
self-managed. Both GitLab SaaS and the self-managed offer the similar capabilities, but they cater to
different needs and come with their own set of advantages and disadvantages. So talking about
the advantages, so within GitLab SaaS, it is easy to use because there is no need of any
infrastructure setup or maintenance. One can get started quickly with minimal technical expertise
and it is also scalable with pay-as-you-go pricing scales seamlessly with your needs.
And you don't need to worry about managing the server capacity. GitLab also manages the
infrastructure, ensuring high uptime and redundancy. And it also gives you automatic
updates, guaranteeing access to the latest features and security patches. And finally,
GitLab handles the security patching as mentioned earlier and also the vulnerability management.
And talking about the pros within the GitLab self-managed instance, you get the full control
on customizing the platform to your specific needs and security requirements. There is no
vendor lock-in. You can easily migrate to another platform or host on your own infrastructure. You
get data privacy where you can maintain complete control over your code and data. And finally,
it is cost-effective for larger teams because potentially it is cheaper when compared with
SaaS for high volume usage. And talking about the challenges, so within GitLab SaaS,
it has limited customizations because you have less control over the platform configurations
compared to the self-hosted GitLab server. And even migrating to another platform can be a complex
and a costly affair. And potential data privacy concerns might arise because your code and data
are hosted on the GitLab infrastructure. And finally, the additional cost for storage and
resources might be expensive for larger teams or projects with high resource demands.
Within GitLab self-managed, you may require technical expertise to manage the infrastructure
and keep the platform updated. Scaling beyond your available infrastructure can be expensive
and complex. And finally, you are responsible for applying the security patches and also managing
any vulnerabilities. So now let's understand how all these three components work together like the
GitLab server, the GitLab runner, and the GitLab executor. And let's see how they interact during
the CI/CD process. So the first is the registration phase. The registration process is the first step
in setting up a GitLab runner. So during the registration, the runner is going to make a
POST request to the runner endpoint of the GitLab server. This request also includes a registration
token, which is a unique identifier provided by the GitLab server. The server is going to in turn
respond with a runner token upon successful registration. This runner token is then used
by the runner for future communications with the GitLab server. The second step is the GitLab
runner is going to request for the jobs and going to handle the jobs. So how is this going to work?
So once the runner is registered, it enters a loop where it continuously requests jobs from the
GitLab server. The runner is going to make a POST request to the request endpoint using the runner
token received during the registration phase. The server is going to respond with a job payload,
which also includes a job token. This job token is used by the runner to authenticate itself when it
performs actions related to the job. The third phase is the job execution phase.
So the job payload received from the server is passed on to the executor. So the executor,
as we discussed, is responsible for executing the job. It's going to use the job token to
authenticate itself when it needs to clone the resources or the sources and the artifacts from
the GitLab server. So the executor runs the job in an environment that is isolated from the runner
and the other jobs. And then we have the final phase where the executor is going to return the
job outputs and the status. So after the job is executed, the executor returns the job output
and the status. So the job token is still used to authenticate these actions. The runner then
updates the job output and status on the GitLab server using the same job token. So this is going
to allow the server to track the progress and the result of the job. So these are the key components
and how they interact within the GitLab CI/CD architecture. Thank you.

============================================================
  13-Executing_Shell_Scripts_in_a_Job
============================================================

Let's understand what happens when you run a pipeline which has multiple dependent jobs.
So here I'm just going to modify the single job and convert it to three separate jobs which are
dependent on each other. So let me just remove this and paste the three jobs. So let me minimize
the jobs and explain what is happening over here. So the job names are build, test, and deploy.
So in the build job, it's going to, you know, install the K8sgpt library and I'm just using
a sleep command to intentionally sleep this job for 30 seconds before running the script.
And within the script, I'm generating, you know, an ASCII artwork and I'm saving it within a file
called as dragon.txt. And in the test job, again, I'm using a script to sleep for 10 seconds
intentionally and then I'm trying to test if the file exists and this word whether the file exists
or not. And finally, I have a deploy job which is again going to read the content of the file
which was generated in the build job, right? So these are the three jobs which are going
to be executed. So let's go ahead and commit the changes. Let's go to the pipeline.
Let's go here and as you can see here, all three jobs are running in parallel.
As we have seen earlier, if you define multiple jobs, by default, all the jobs
will be executed in parallel and they do not wait for the other jobs. So if you see here,
these jobs are running in a random fashion. So as per the visuals, you have the build,
deploy and the test job. So let's just wait for a few seconds and see what happens.
So let me refresh this page and you can see here that the deploy job has failed first.
And the reason is both the build and the test jobs have a sleep command which, you know,
sleeps for a few seconds. And now even the test job failed, but still the build job is still
happening. Okay. So let's go to the build job.
And now even the build job is successfully completed. And as you can see here, it executed
and installed the Kausai library and it slept for 30 seconds. And then it created,
you know, the ASCII artwork and created it in a file called as Dragon.txt.
So the build job is success. But what about the other jobs, the test and the deploy jobs?
So these jobs failed because they could not access the Dragon.txt file, which was created
in the build job. So if you remember, all these three jobs run on three different virtual machines
and hence they cannot share the same file that was created in some other job. Right.
And moreover, the order of execution is also in a random fashion because they run in parallel.
So ideally speaking, first the build job has to be completed, then the test job should start
and it should be able to access the file which was created in the build job. And finally, after
the test job completed successfully, the deploy job should start and execute it. Since the order
was not followed here, the other two jobs are failing because they could not access the file
which was generated or created in the build job. So in the next session, we'll try to address
these issues. That's all for now. Thank you.

  17-Using_needs_Keyword
============================================================

Let's have a quick look at how we can use variables at different levels within the pipeline.
So within our existing pipeline, if you see at the jobs of the Docker build, testing and the push,
you see that we are making use of the same values in different jobs. And if you, you know,
use multiple jobs for the same settings, it would be better if you define them using variables and
reuse them in multiple jobs, right? So how can you define variables within the pipeline?
So if you go back to the syntax reference, and if you go to variables at the bottom,
so variables can be defined at the global and at the job level as well. And if you define variables
at both levels, so the job level variable takes higher precedence. And if you want to
understand how variables are defined, so in this example, this variable is defined at the global
level. So you need to use the keyword variables, and you can define your variable key and the value.
And in order to refer to this variable within the job, so this global variable will be available
to all the jobs within the pipeline. So you can refer to the variable using dollar and the key of
the variable. So that is how you can define and use variables. So now let's modify our Docker
build jobs to make use of variables at the job level. So I'll just copy-paste a few commands
instead of typing and spending a lot of time on it. So just to save some time, I just copy-pasted,
you know, the same jobs, but with variables. So if you see here, I created a job-level variable,
I'm using four variables: username, registry, image, and version. And in order to use these
variables within the script, I'm just using the dollar, you know, syntax, the dollar syntax,
and the name of the key. So here I created a username variable. And I'm also referring to that
variable within another variable within the registry variable. So even that level of dependencies can
be used at the variables also. Okay, so this same set of variables is also used in the testing job
as well. And the same set of variables is also used at the Docker push job. But one additional
variable at the push job is the password, which is required for, you know, logging into the Docker
hub. Right. So again, using variables, we are repeating the same variables across multiple jobs.
So is there a better way to reuse variables across multiple jobs? Yes, we can define
commonly used variables at the global level as well. So what we can do here is, so let me just
copy up to the version. So I can go to the top. So let me just minimize the other job.
So at the global level, I'll just define the variables and, you know, use these variables.
And how do I refer to them? Right. So these are automatically referred to in all the jobs. So
wherever you have the same values, so you can, you know, simply remove them. So I can remove

  19-Masking_Variables_using_Project_Settings
============================================================

Let's have a look at predefined CI/CD variables.
Predefined CI/CD variables have completely separate documentation which you can go through
for different examples. So these are the variables which are automatically provided by GitLab in
every CI/CD pipeline. They contain information about the job, pipeline, and other useful values
relevant to your builds and deployments. So this allows you to write flexible and dynamic scripts
without hardcoding any sensitive information directly in your GitLab-CI YAML file.
And talking about the availability of these variables. So some variables are available
as soon as GitLab creates the pipeline, allowing you to use them in the pipeline configuration
or in any job scripts. And some other variables become available only when a runner executes the
job, meaning that they can only be used within the job script. Apart from this, there are also
different variables which are only accessible during a merge request or during an external
pull request. So we'll be exploring them in one of our examples as we move forward.
So you have like I think hundreds of variables available. So you can quickly go through this
and understand how they work. Apart from that, if you want to quickly see how the variables can be
accessed within your job, you can also use something called an export command, which is
going to list all the variables available to the script using the export command.

============================================================
  20-Exploring_Predefined_CICD_Variables
============================================================

So what are GitLab Merge Requests? A merge request lets you propose
merging changes from a source branch to a target branch. Does this remind you of anything?
Both GitLab Merge Requests and GitHub Pull Requests serve the same purpose.
They propose changes from a branch to be merged into the main branch of a code repository.
However, they differ in terminology and some workflow implementation details.
But both of these concepts allow the developers to create branching points for the changes,
commit proposed codes like code documentation, etc., for review, discuss changes with the team
and receive the feedback or approvals in some cases. And finally, the changes get reviewed and
potentially merged back into the main codebase. So in the upcoming sessions, we are going to make
use of this merge request concept in GitLab for merging the changes from one branch to another
branch. Thank you.

  24-Pipeline_Schedules

Let's have a look at resource groups.
In GitLab CI, multiple pipelines run concurrently.
But in some cases, such as the deployment cases, you don't want to run the jobs concurrently and you want to run them one by one.
So this is the place where resource groups come into the picture.
So if you want to know more about Resource Groups, you can go to the CI/CD YAML syntax reference and search for Resource Groups.
So you can use a Resource Group to create a group that ensures that a job is mutually exclusive across different pipelines for the same project.
For example, if multiple jobs that belong to the same resource group are queued simultaneously, only one of the jobs starts.
The other job waits until the resource group is free.
And again, you have various process modes which can be used to strategically control the job concurrency.
And the default one is unordered.
So let's check out what are the different modes available and then we'll check out a demo as well.
So let's check out the different process modes.
So we have three types of process modes, unordered, oldest first, and newest first.
And the default process is unordered where the execution order of the jobs is unordered, which means that any job can start first and the others will be queued.
So let's take an example and try to understand these three process modes.
So if you come down, there is an example where you have two jobs, the build and the deploy job.
And let's assume you have done three commits to the same pipeline.
So what happens here is in the first pipeline, it's going to run both the build and the deploy job.
So let's call this as deploy one.
Similarly, the second and the third pipelines are called as deploy two and deploy three.
So we have three deployments.
So depending on the process mode, this is how it works.
So within the unordered process mode, since we have these three deployments, they do not run concurrently.
So and there is no guarantee on the job execution order.
So for example, job, you know, deploy one could run before or after the deploy three or the deploy two.
That's the unordered mode.
And whereas coming to the oldest first mode.
So if you have deploy one, deploy two, deploy three.
So deploy one is going to run first, then deploy two, followed by deploy three.
So that is how the oldest first works.
And the opposite of this is the newest first.
So in the newest first, it's first going to run the deploy three, then the deploy two, followed by the deploy one.
So these are the three modes available.
And the default mode is the unordered.
If you want to change the mode, you need to make use of the GitLab CI REST API to change the mode.
Okay, so now let's try to use the Resource Groups in the unordered fashion.
So in order to use a resource group, so this is what you need to mention in your job.
So let me just copy this, go to my pipeline.
And I would like to just mention the resource group for my deploy job.
And the value could be anything.
I'm just using production as an example, but it could be any value.
Okay, so let's quickly commit this to the main branch.
And I also want to add a sleep command as well, just to show you how this works.
And I'm just going to add a sleep of 300 seconds.
Okay, it's around five minutes.
So let me just commit this to the main branch.
So if I go to the pipelines, a new pipeline should be triggered.
So if you see a new pipeline is triggered, and it is running.
At the same time, if I schedule a pipeline manually, so let's go to pipeline schedules.
And let me, you know, run the pipeline manually.
Okay.
So again, this is going to run for the same main branch, right?
So if I go to the pipelines, and if you see, there are two pipelines running,
whereas the second pipeline is waiting.
The reason it is waiting is the deploy job is already running in the first pipeline,
which I triggered when I committed it to the main branch.
So if you see the deploy job is running, since the deploy job is already running,
which has a resource group keyword available.
So any other job which is trying to run the deploy job or any other pipeline,
which is trying to run the deploy job will be in the waiting stage.
So if I go back to my pipelines, since the scheduled job is also trying to execute the same deploy job,
it will be in the waiting state.
If you see it is in waiting state.
So if I open this, it's going to give you additional information saying that,
you know, this job is waiting for resource production.
So any jobs which are using the resource production should complete
or should be canceled in order to start this waiting job.
So let's do one thing.
So let's go to our pipeline and cancel the first pipeline.
Okay. So how do you cancel the pipeline?
So if you want to cancel the pipeline, you can simply click on cancel the running pipeline.
Or if you want to just cancel a specific job within the pipeline,
you can also cancel the job as well.
So you can do either of them in this scenario because we only have one job running.
So as soon as I cancel this pipeline, this job should automatically trigger.
So let me cancel the first pipeline.
So this is canceled.
And if you see here, automatically this job gets triggered because the other job was canceled
and the resource group production can accept another deployment.
So that is how you can make use of the Resource Groups.
So that you can control the concurrency of the job trigger points.
So if I go back to the pipelines,
so you should see that the pipeline which is canceled has an icon canceled
and the pipeline or the scheduled pipeline is in a running state now.
Okay. So that's about the resource groups.
And in the next session, we'll talk about how you can make use of job timeouts as well.
That's all for now. Thank you.

  25-Resource_Groups
============================================================

Let's talk about job timeout.
While working with jobs, script errors or dependency issues can sometimes indefinitely run job executions, causing pipelines to stall and consume resources.
In such cases, implementing timeouts automatically terminates such jobs, optimizing resource utilization and pipeline execution speeds.
So how do you configure timeout for a specific job?
So you can go back to the CI/CD YAML syntax reference and search for timeout.
So you can configure a timeout for a specific job and it's going to fail the job if the job runs longer than the timeout.
And you can define the timeout using natural language like 3600 seconds, 1 hour.
It could be also like 3H30M, which translates to 3 hours and 30 minutes.
So all you need to do is use the timeout keyword along with the time unit which you want to use.
So let's go back and this job generally takes more than 5 minutes to complete because we are using a sleep command.
So what I can do with this, so I can use a timeout keyword and I just want this job to complete within 10 seconds.
If the job doesn't complete within 10 seconds, I want to terminate the job.
So that's what the timeout keyword is going to do.
So let's go ahead and commit the changes.
So let's go and check out the pipeline. So I can click on the view pipeline here.
Right. A new job has kicked in.
And this job will only wait for 10 seconds.
If it takes more than 10 seconds, it's going to, you know, simply fail the job.
And as you can see here, this job failed right in pulling the Docker image for Ruby 3.1.
Okay. It's saying that, you know, context deadline exceeded because we just gave it 10 seconds.
Right. So as you can see the time over here.
So it worked for 10 seconds and on the 11th second, it just terminated the job because of the timeout keyword, which I used.
So it's going to just fail the job and it's going to say that there has been a runner system failure.
Please try again. Right. So this is how you can use a timeout function.
I just used 10 seconds as an example to show how quickly I can, you know, fail the build.
But this value should be a meaningful value as per your job and you know how much time is required for it.
Right. So that's how the job timeout works.
Thank you.

In this session, let's go ahead and understand the GitLab CI's parallel keyword. So, let's take
an example. So, let's assume you have an extensive test suite of more than 100 test cases, which has
to be executed. And if you have a single test job, which is running all these 100 test cases,
it may take a lot of time to complete the unit testing or any other testing. So,
let's assume it's taking you around 20 minutes to run all those 100 test cases. So, this is the place
where the parallelization comes into picture. So, using the GitLab CI's parallel keyword,
you can significantly reduce the pipeline's duration. So, if you go to the CI/CD YAML syntax
reference, so there is a parallel keyword. So, this keyword, or this can be used to run a job
multiple times in parallel in a single pipeline. So, GitLab allows you to split your work into
multiple jobs and then run concurrently, improving the overall pipeline speed. So,
let's assume if you have 100 test cases, so you can use a parallel job of 10 jobs,
and you can split those 100 test cases across these 10 jobs. So, each job is going to run 10
test cases. So, overall, at the end of the day, it would highly reduce your pipeline duration from
20 minutes to let's say 10 minutes or 15 minutes, right? So, that's how the parallelization works
in GitLab CI. Apart from the parallel keyword, we also have something called as the parallel
matrix. So, using this matrix, you can also run jobs multiple times in parallel within the single
pipeline, but using different variable values. So, let's try to understand this with an example.
So, if I go back to my pipeline editor, so here I'm trying to run this deploy job or basically
to get the versions of NodeJS and npm by running this on a specific node image.
What if I want to deploy or test the same thing on multiple node images or on multiple versions,
and also on multiple different random machines? One way is to simply copy-pasting the same thing
and changing the versions. So, again, this would create two jobs, and it would run on two different
Docker images. But this is not efficient if you have a larger set of things to test.
So, this is a place where you can make use of the parallel matrix. So, how can you use that?
So, you can use the parallel keyword, and you can also use a matrix. So, you have to define
a set of variables here. So, first, let me define the random machines. So, I would like to run this
job on a couple of different machines. So, let me just copy random machines. And let me just run
this on, let us say, the SAST small Linux machine and the SAST medium machine. So, let me just paste
the values. So, I would like to run this job on these two machines. And if you want, you can also
use Windows and Mac OS as well. So, you have to give the right tags. So, once you define this
matrix, so if you want to make use of these variables, of course, you need to make use of
tags in order to, you know, associate this job with a specific random machine. So, here, earlier,
we were hard coding the value, but now you should not be hard coding. So, you should be getting the
values, you know, from the random machines parallel matrix variable. So, how do I get that?
So, I can make use of dollar and I need to use the runner machine. So, as soon as I do this,
so now this job is going to run four times on these four different random machines. So, let me see if
I can also save this and visualize. Okay, it's not showing me over here. Let's see if I can save
this and validate. So, let's validate the pipeline. And as you can see here, it is showing me four
separate jobs. So, each job is going to run on a different random machine. But ideally speaking,
in the actual editor, we only have a single job, but using the parallel matrix, it's going to run
the job on four different random machines. So, let me see if this can also be visualized in full
config. No, it won't visualize here. But yes, in validate, you can see that it's showing you four
different jobs. Okay. So, this is like a single level of matrix, but I only want to run this on
the two machines like the Linux machines. So, I'll just remove the Windows and the macOS.
So, apart from this, let's say I also want to run this job for different NodeJS versions to test it
or to deploy it on different NodeJS versions. So, how do I do that? So, within the same matrix,
I can define another variable. I can give this any name. I'm just using node version
as the name or node versions. And here again, I can give an array of versions.
So, I would just like to give three versions over here. So, let me quickly copy the versions.
Right. So, I would like to run this on the version 18, 20 and 21. So, again,
I need to copy this and use a dollar value to refer to the node versions. Right. So, now,
this single job will be run six times in parallel because we have two runner machines
and three node versions. So, each version will be running on both the machines. So,
since we have three versions and two machines, three multiplied by two is six. So, a total of six
jobs will be running in parallel using the single configuration. I think we can also see this within
the validation. If I validate the pipeline, I should be seeing six jobs. Right. So, each job is using
small. So, let me just use small. For the small machine, it's going to run all the three versions
20, 18, 21. And for the medium machines, again, it's going to use the same node versions.
So, I think I made one more small mistake over here. So, I need to use
node: the version, because I'm only dynamically changing the version. I'm not
changing the image name. I'm only changing the tag. So, I can say tags instead of version. I can say
tags. So, let's try to commit this. Let's go to Pipeline.
A new pipeline got started. And here we have a single job. But if you see there is a number six.
So, if you try to hover your mouse on top of it, it's going to show you that there are six jobs
which are triggered in parallel. And all the jobs will be running in parallel. If you click on jobs,
you can see the same thing. But the thing is, some of the jobs are in a waiting state because
the runners might not be available at the time. So, as soon as the runners are available,
it's going to execute the remaining jobs as well. For now, let's go to this job which has

version is 21. So, if I scroll down, it should show you the NodeJS version as 21.
Okay. So, that's how you can parallelize your jobs. So, I just used the parallel matrix job
to show you how to use multiple variables and run multiple jobs in parallel. If you don't want to
use variables, you can simply use the parallel keyword and run your jobs in parallel across multiple
different jobs, so that you can quickly run the test cases by splitting them across multiple jobs.
So, that's all for now. Thank you.

============================================================
  28-Parallel_Matrix
============================================================

So, let's understand why we need to skip a pipeline and how to skip it if required.
So, let's say in some scenarios, you might not make any changes to the workflow editor or the pipeline,
or you might not make any changes to the code within your repository,
but you're making some small changes in the README file or you're making some changes in the .gitignore file.
So, in those cases, you don't want to execute or trigger a pipeline because that is irrelevant to your pipeline code.
So, how do you skip pipeline triggers in those scenarios?
So, this is where you can skip a pipeline.
So, whenever you're pushing a commit and if you don't want to trigger a pipeline,
you can add a [skip ci] or [ci skip] within the commit message and it won't trigger a pipeline.
So, let's try to quickly check this out.
So, let me go to the README file.
Let me edit this file.
So, let's say testing skip pipeline and in the commit message, if I commit this, this is going to trigger the pipeline.
But ideally speaking, I did not make any changes in the code or any pipeline changes.
So, triggering a pipeline for this scenario is illogical.
So, you can simply skip the pipeline trigger using the skip CI.
You can just mention this in the commit message and commit it and this won't trigger any pipeline.
So, if you see here in this icon, it simply says that the pipeline is skipped.
So, if you go to the pipelines tab, build pipelines tab, it's going to show you the status.
It's saying that for this specific commit, the pipeline was skipped.
You won't see any jobs.
It's simply saying that the pipeline is skipped over here.
So, this is how you can skip pipelines when you don't want them to be triggered for specific commits within your repository.
Thank you.

Project Status Meeting
Over the course of this project, we will be developing a GitHub workflow for a NodeJS
application. To simplify understanding and making the process more streamlined,
we will utilize this Project Status Meeting slides. These slides will present an overview
of the tasks we will be performing and the reason behind the changes. Additionally,
comments will be provided wherever necessary to further clarify the context.

============================================================
  30-Project_Status_Meeting_-_1
============================================================

Let's take a brief look into Node.js.
In a later model, we will utilize the Node.js application to develop a customized GitHub Action workflow.
Node.js is an open-source runtime environment that enables developers to execute code outside of web browsers.
With Node.js, the developers can create both front-end and back-end applications using JavaScript.
NodeJS is built on top of Chrome V8 JavaScript engine.
This makes it possible to build back-end applications using the same JavaScript programming language you may be familiar with.
NodeJS can be installed on all major platforms including Windows, macOS, and various Linux distributions.
So within NodeJS, when we install it, npm also gets installed.
So npm stands for Node Package Manager.
It is a package manager for JavaScript and NodeJS applications.
It is used to discover, share, distribute, and manage the various packages, libraries, and dependencies that JavaScript developers use when building web and server-side applications.
So let's explore a sample NodeJS project to learn how to run the test and execute NodeJS applications.
Mind you, this is a very basic minimal NodeJS application that displays a simple hello world.
So we have a package.json file in a NodeJS project which contains the metadata including the essential information about the project like its name, version, dependencies, and the scripts.
It is used for dependency management, enabling you to specify which external packages your project relies on developing and deploying your application.
To install the dependencies defined within the package.json, we run the npm install command.
Once the install is successful, a node_modules directory gets created which contains all the external JavaScript modules and packages that your project depends on.
Finally, we have the index.js file where the actual business logic exists and a test.js file is used to define the test cases.
So we have an npm test command which is commonly used to execute all the test cases for a JavaScript project.
This command runs all the tests defined within the test.js file.
Finally, in a NodeJS project, we generally use the npm start command to start your application.
And once the application is started, we can access it on the browser by making a call on the port number.
Thank you.

============================================================
  32-Run_and_Test_NodeJS_App_on_Local_Machine
============================================================

Let's understand the requirement of the DevOps pipeline.
The team wants to host all the code on the GitLab repository.
So once the code is committed to the repository, we want to have a pipeline which starts by
doing a round of unit testing using the npm install to install the dependencies,
npm test for, you know, running the test cases, and also to download the reports.
And if there are any errors during the unit test cases, we would like to, you know,
stop the pipeline. If there are no errors, we would like to continue
by executing the code coverage step. So within the code coverage step,
we also want to run both unit testing and code coverage in parallel.
So we have to also, you know, reinstall the npm packages using npm install command,
and, you know, run the code coverage using the npm run coverage command.
And even in the code coverage, we would like to, you know, download the generated reports.
And there is an exception for the code coverage stage. So if there are any errors for the code coverage,
we would like to ignore them for the time being and continue processing the pipeline.
So as part of the next step, we would like to have a job which is going to
do the containerization for us. So it's going to use Docker for building
Docker images, running the Docker image and also, you know, pushing it to a container registry.
And if we have any errors in the Docker stage, we want to stop the pipeline here itself.
And then if there are no errors, we want to go ahead
and deploy the application to the dev instance of a K8s cluster.
So we'll be using the manifest file. So we'll directly apply them using kubectl apply.
It also has an Ingress URL. So we'll get the Ingress.
So in the next stage, we'll do a round of integration testing by hitting the Ingress URL
endpoint, which we received from the previous stage.
And at this point, if you have any errors, we'll just stop the pipeline and not proceed further.
If there are no errors, before we deploy it to the production instance, we'll be having
or we want to have a manual approval stage where, you know, a developer or a reviewer
has to review all the artifacts and he or she can either approve it or decline it.
So if it is declined, the pipeline will stop here itself.
Or if it is approved, it goes to the next step, which is, you know,
deploying it to the production Kubernetes instance.
So where we'll be running the same set of commands.
And after the deployment is successful, we'll also test it using a bunch of integration tests,
which basically calls the ingress endpoint and, you know, checks some status.
Right.
So this is the DevOps pipeline, which we will be creating for the NodeJS application
using GitHub Action workflows.
That's all for now.
Thank you.

  37-Pipeline_-_Configure_Coverage_and_Archive_Reports

Let's go ahead and understand how to ignore job failures and continue processing the other jobs
within the pipeline. So if you remember, in the previous job, when the code coverage job failed,
it skipped the other job which was dependent on it. But in some cases, you know, you might want
to ignore a job failure and continue processing the next job. So how to do that? If you go back
to the CI/CD YAML syntax reference, so we have a keyword called allow_failure, which determines
whether a pipeline should continue running when a job fails. So you can simply use allow_failure as
true. And even if the job fails, it continues running the next jobs within the pipeline.
And else you can use the default value, which is the false option. And when you use the allow
failure as true, if the job fails, it's going to show you a warning, but the pipeline will
be successfully marked as success. And you can also check out a few examples over here. So within
the job, you just need to use the syntax allow_failure: true. And you can also have some
customizations. So if you want to only allow the failure of a specific exit code, you can also do
that. So if you scroll down, there is an option where you can control when a job should be allowed
to fail using the exit codes as well. So if you see this example, by default, this is going to be
allow_failure: true, but this job will only be allowed to fail if the exit code is 137.
As you can see here, in this script, we are by default setting it to one. So this job will not
be allowed to fail. So if it exits, it's going to be you know, treated as a pipeline failure.
So you can also use an array of exit codes and any one of the exit codes should match with the
job's exit code. So that is how you can make use of this allow failures keyword to either allow
the job to fail and continue processing, or you can also decide based on the exit code as well.
So let's go ahead and use this within our pipeline. So within the code coverage job,
I'll just use the allow failure as true. So I don't want to use the exit code option for the
time being. So if you want, you can, you know, configure that as well. So let me just say, add it.
Okay, and let me quickly commit this to the feature branch.
So let's go to the pipeline. So a new pipeline has been triggered. Okay, so as you can see here,
now the job has completed, even the pipeline has been completed. But you see there are a couple of
warnings over here. So the sample job has been successfully executed, even when the code coverage
job failed. So if you hover the mouse on the code coverage job, you see that it's failed
with a warning sign. And for the pipeline, it's also successful, but it's going to have this
warning sign just to say that, you know, one of the jobs has failed. Okay,
so let's go to this code coverage job. You can still see that the job has failed with exit code
one. But this has been ignored because we have used allow_failure as true. And since it was
ignored, the next job, which was the sample job, which was dependent on the code coverage job,
on the code coverage job was also triggered, and it also completed successfully.
Since the jobs have been completed successfully, the pipeline will be treated as successful,
but it's going to have this warning icon displayed here. So if you go to the pipelines tab,
so if you see earlier pipelines showed just passed or failed, but our current pipeline
is showing us a warning, which means that, you know, one of the jobs has passed with some warnings.
