  39-Exploring_Reports_through_Merge_Request
============================================================

Let's go ahead and talk about why caching dependencies is important and how
to cache the dependencies in the GitLab CI pipeline. So if you remember, we are
making use of a NodeJS based application and most of the programming
languages depend upon some dependencies in order to write the code.
So within Node.js, we make use of the package.json file to define the dependencies
required for our application. So if you see here, I'm using around 5 to 9
different dependencies for my application because it's a very simple
one. But more complex applications have, you know, if not tens or hundreds of
dependencies. And in order to run the application, first you need to install
the dependencies and then those can be used, you know, during the application
runtime. And in Node.js, when you try to, you know, install the dependencies, it will
be also creating a package-lock.json file, which contains all the
details regarding the dependencies and it's also going to log their versions as
a security measure. Okay, so that's about the package.json and how many
dependencies we have for this application. And if I go back to my
pipeline, if you remember, we are currently using two jobs, the unit
testing and the code coverage job. And in both the jobs, before you run the jobs, we
are installing the dependencies using npm install. So let's see how much time
it's taking to install these dependencies in both the jobs.
So here, if you see in the unit testing job, it took around 7 seconds to install the
dependencies. So each dependency has multiple packages. So currently, you know,
it's adding around 364 packages and it's taking around 7 seconds.
And since we have only 9 dependencies, the time is in seconds. If you have hundreds of
dependencies, the time does increase. It also goes in minutes as well. So the same
thing is also happening in the code coverage job. So if you go to code
coverage job, even it requires the same set of dependencies. Since it is running
on a different, you know, runner machine, again, it's going to, you know, add the
same packages and this time it took around 6 seconds, right? So if you have
multiple jobs, which require the same set of dependencies, and if you try to
run the jobs multiple times, each time it has to add or download and install the
same set of dependencies and it's going to waste your time. So to avoid this and
to improve this, we have the caching capability in GitLab. So you can cache
one or more files when a job gets executed and we can make use of this
cache in the same pipeline within the different jobs or you can also use the
same, you know, cache across multiple pipelines. So cache might sound similar
to artifacts but it is a bit different. So we use cache for dependencies like
packages which you download from the internet and use in your project.
So this will be cached on the runner and it can also be uploaded to AWS S3 if required.
Whereas artifacts are created when the job executes and this can be used in the
intermediate jobs within the same pipeline. So that's the major difference
between caching and artifacts. So how do you configure the cache? So the cache can
be configured for each job using the cache keyword. So if you want to know
more about the cache keyword, we will explore the syntax reference. But before that,
this, you know, documentation also explains, you know, how you can use the cache for
different programming languages like NodeJS, PHP, Python, Ruby and Go as well.
So now let's go ahead and see what options we have in the cache keyword.
So if I go back to the CI/CD YAML syntax reference, we have this cache option and
it has various keys associated with it. So you don't need to use all the keys
but we'll use some of them. Okay. So one important thing is cache can be
shared between multiple jobs and pipelines as well. Okay. So let's scroll
down and check out the path. So you need to define the path which needs to be
cached. Okay. So it could be a single file or it could be an array of files.
So you have to use the paths in order to define what has to be cached. And you can
also use a key. So the keyword is, it's going to give you a unique key for each
cache. So multiple jobs using the same cache key use the same cache.
And it also, you know, is the same for different pipelines as well. So as long as the
key is the same, it's going to download the same cache and use it across jobs
or across the pipelines. And you can define the key using a string or a
predefined CI/CD variable or a combination of both of them. So instead
of using a single key, you can also associate the key with a file as well.
So when you're associating the key with the files, the keyword or the, you
know, the unique key will be generated based on the file changes. So whenever
you, you know, make a change to the file, it's going to generate a unique SHA
value and that is going to be used as the cache key. So here, I mean, if you're
working with Ruby applications, you can use the Gemfile.lock as the key file, or
if you're making use of, you know, Node.js applications, you can use package.json
or package-lock.json because whenever you make any changes in
the dependencies, you either make the change in the package.json and when
you install them, the change would be reflected in the package-lock.json,
right? So you can use these files as a key in case of, you know, Node.js based
applications. And apart from the, you know, keys, you can also add a prefix so
that you can give that key a more meaningful name if required. So that is
an optional thing. And apart from that, you have the policy option. So if you
don't define the policy, by default, it's going to use the push and the pull
policy. So what is the use of the policy? So this policy defines what the job has
to do in case of a cache. Does it require to download the cache or upload
the cache? So that is defined using the policy key. So by default, it's going to,
you know, first pull if any cache is available and then push cache which was
created in this job. So apart from pull and push, which is a default option, you
can only use the pull option, which is only going to download the cache when
the job starts, but it's never going to upload the changes when the job finishes.
And similarly, if you use only the push as the policy, it's only going to upload
the cache when the job finishes, but it's never going to download the cache when
the job starts. Okay, so these are the different keys available. So now let's go
ahead and add cache to our unit testing and the code coverage jobs. So you can add
cache, you know, before the script stage. Right, so I'll just add cache. So let me
make some changes. So I'll just use policy as pull-push and when it's on
success. So only when the unit test job successfully completes, it's going to, you
know, push the cache. Else, it's not going to push. It's only going to pull the
cache. Okay, so that's a strategy, you know, which I'm using. So coming to the
path. So what is the path which I would like to, you know, cache? If you remember,
as soon as you do npm install, it's going to create a node_modules
directory, which contains all your dependencies. So this is the folder which
I would like to, you know, cache. So I can simply use node_modules.
And mind you, this path should be a directory or a file which should be available at
the local Git repository, which was available for the runner. Okay, so that's
the path. And what should be the key, right? So for the key path, I would like
to use the files option. Okay, so let's use the files option for the key.

avoiding to reinstall the same packages across multiple jobs, or
across multiple pipelines. Okay. And again, it's going to, you know,

approach. So if you go to pipelines, so there is a clear runner cache
option, which is going to clear the cache from all the runners. But you
don't want to clear the cache manually, you want to clear the cache
whenever there is a change in the dependencies. So whenever someone adds
or modifies one of the dependencies, it needs to remove the cache and, you

============================================================
  42-Project_Status_Meeting_-_2
============================================================

Let's talk about GitLab CI/CD image and service options.
When creating a pipeline, we are given the choice to select a runner for each job.
Often, we opt for GitLab hosted runners to execute our workflows.
For instance, here the runner's tag has a value of Ubuntu latest.
It is going to select a runner with Ubuntu operating system,
and these runners come with pre-installed software containing the language runtimes,
package management tools, command-line utilities, web browsers, test-suite docker images,
and much more. And within a job, we can directly
leverage these pre-installed software components to carry out our steps.
So let's take an example. When we opt for this GitLab hosted Linux-based
SAST runner, it defaults to the Ruby 3.1 container. This provides a pre-configured environment for
Ruby projects out of the box. This reduces complexity for teams primarily working with
Ruby. It's going to simplify managing the runner configurations and focuses on writing code.
But what about non-Ruby applications or projects? Accidentally triggering a pipeline in this default
container might lead to confusion and unexpected errors due to the presence of Ruby-specific tools.
Sometimes, there are instances when we need a different version of a runtime
or a package that isn't readily available in the pre-installed environment.
In such cases, we can compose additional steps in our workflow
to install the required runtime versions and packages.
So in this given example, we are installing NodeJS Runtime version 20 using a before script
for our unit testing job. When we trigger the pipeline, it's going to provision a GitLab
hosted runner running the Linux operating system. It will first prepare and start a Ruby container
and all the steps are executed within this container. It is worth noting that in certain
situations, the time it takes to install specific runtimes and packages can significantly increase
the GitLab CI/CD billing cost. To mitigate this, one effective strategy is to use the image containers.
In GitLab CI/CD, image containers play a crucial role in defining the environment your job runs in.
Image containers are like pre-built sandboxes for your code to run in.
Each job gets its own isolated container with the specific tools and environment it needs.
For example, it could be like for Python libraries for a web application or NodeJS
for a backend service. So these containers provide isolation with tools or conflicting
dependencies between jobs. It's going to help you with reproducibility where jobs always run
the same way regardless of the runner machine and you can also control on choosing the exact
tools and versions for each job for optimal efficiency and security. To utilize an image
container, you must specify the Docker image to be used within the job by configuring the images
keyword. Additionally, you also have the flexibility to define other configurations
including pull policies, entry point and others. For instance, this job uses the image keyword to
execute a container that includes NodeJS runtime version 20. By utilizing this container,
the need to install the NodeJS runtime separately is eliminated. During the execution
of the workflow, GitLab is going to provision a hosted runner with the Linux operating system.
Within this operating system, it's going to create and run the NodeJS Docker container
and executing all the defined steps within that container. So let's assume that in this particular
job, unit tests are executed while connected to the production database. This practice is not
advisable as it can potentially impact the production database performance. So for tasks
like unit testing or code coverage analysis, it is recommended to use a dedicated testing
database or services. GitLab CI/CD offers another solution to address this issue as well. Much like
the job image containers, service containers within GitLab CI/CD are Docker containers that
provide a simple and portable way for you to host services. They provide specific resources
and capabilities that your job might need during execution. So we can think of them as specialized
tools on your workbench, such as providing resources for testing purposes. A service can
spin up a temporary MySQL instance for your job. You can also boost your job capabilities by using
a Docker-in-Docker service. You can increase the flexibility by creating custom services,
allowing you to tailor them for your specific needs. And finally, like job containers,
services run in their own environment, which prevents interference with your main jobs.
So service images are specified using the service keyword. This additional image is
used to create another container, which is available to the first container. So the two
containers have access to one another and can communicate when running the job. This pipeline
configuration specifies a job that runs all the steps within a NodeJS based image container.
It utilizes the Linux GitLab hosted runner as the Docker host for this container.
Additionally, the job is configured with a service container with a custom MongoDB Docker image
and an alias Mongo. When the pipeline is executed, it will run two containers. One is the
MongoDB service container, which runs MongoDB and populates the test data. And within the job
container, it carries out the following steps. It checks out the repository of the runner,
installs the dependencies, executes a unit test by connecting to the MongoDB service container.
But how does the unit testing step execute the job container connect with MongoDB in
the service container? So given that the job and the service Docker containers
run within the same user-defined bridge network, all ports are exposed to each other. You will
have seamless access to the service container via the default MongoDB port, which is 27017.
So the host name of the MongoDB service corresponds to the alias configured in the
service YAML, which is in this case, Mongo. So now that we have an idea about
job and service containers, let's go ahead and implement them within our pipeline. Thank you.

============================================================
  43-What_are_Services
============================================================

Let's go ahead and configure services within a workflow.
Before that, let me just recap some details about services.
As mentioned earlier, services are Docker containers which provide a simple and portable
way for you to host services that might need to test or operate your applications in a Pipeline.
You know, for an example, your pipeline might need to run unit tests that require access to
a database or some memory cache, right? So you're not only limited to using database services,
any image found at Docker Hub or your private container registry can be used as a service.
But before using them, I also want to highlight what services are not used for.
So as mentioned earlier, this feature is designed to provide network accessible services
and a database is the simplest example of such a service. And if you try to, you know,
add any programming languages like PHP, NodeJS, Golang within the services, your script cannot
access the commands of these specific programming languages. So in order to access the CLI or the
commands of the specific languages, you need to define them as the job image, not within the
services. Okay, so that is a major distinction between the job images and the service images.
And talking about services, so how do you define them? So you can simply use the services tag and you
can define the image which you want to use. And you can start using that service, you know,
within the script. So how do you connect with the service? So you can connect to the service by,
you know, just hitting the hostname. So let's take this example. So let's assume you defined
a service to term / WordPress:latest. So how do you, how does your script connect with
this service? So by default, GitLab is going to create two, you know, hostnames for us.
So the first thing is, it's going to replace the slash by two or double underscores.
Or in the second host name, it's going to replace the slash by a single dash. And whatever,
you know, it's after the colon, it is completely ignored. And by the way, using the underscores
is not a valid RFC. And it may cause some problems when you try to connect it with some third-party
applications. So it's always recommended to connect to services using aliases. So what are
the different options which you can define in a service? So you can go and check the available
settings for services. So the first thing which is required is the name. So this is the full name of
the image to be used. And if you are not using any alias, so the name will be converted into
a host name. As discussed earlier, it's going to replace the slash with a single dash or double
underscore. After that, you can also define an entry point and a command which will be provided
to the container. And these are equivalent to the Docker, you know, definitions of Docker entry point
and the Docker command. Apart from that, you can provide alias as I've told you earlier. So by
providing an alias, it becomes easier to access service by just, you know, referring to the alias
name. And apart from that, if you want to pass some additional environment variables to the
container or to the service which is running the container, you can also assign variables at the
service level as well. So to see additional information about services, you can go to CI/CD
YAML syntax reference, you can search for services, it gives you some other additional information,
and you know how to define the services. It also has Docker option and where you can define the
platform and the user and you can also define the pull policies. Okay, so that's about a brief
recap about services. So let's try to configure services within our pipeline for the unit testing
and the code coverage jobs. So let me just go back to the unit testing.
So I'll just define services and I would like to define the name. So here I will be making use
of one of my Docker image which is specifically created for this use case. So this Docker image
is hosted on my Docker Hub account and the name of the image is MongoDB non-prod. So this image
contains a MongoDB and it also populates the database with my solar system, you know,
details like all the planet details. So everything is built in this single image
and you can also give it an alias. So let me give it an alias
Mongo. And you can also use a pull policy as well. So let's say I would like to add a pull policy
as always. So it's going to always, you know, download this image if you're running this on a
Self-Managed Runner. Okay, so that's how you define a service. But how does your unit testing job,
you know, access the service? So if you see here, the unit testing job also runs within a container
because we are making use of the image option. So now you have two containers running within
the same runner. So these two containers will have access to one another and can communicate
when the job is in running state. So in order to connect with the service, you can define the
variables at the job level. So let me just define a set of variables at the job level.
So here for the Mongo URI, I need to connect to the MongoDB which is running in the service
container. So how do I connect to it? So the first thing is I can just use a MongoDB colon
slash slash. And for the host name, I need to be using the alias. Okay, since I defined the alias,
I can simply use Mongo. If I did not define the alias, I should be making use of
the complete image name. So anything after colon, it will be stripped off. So and the slash,
it could be you could use either a single dash or double underscore. So this would be the host
name if you're not using an alias. But since I defined an alias, I'll just go with Mongo.
And I also need to give a port. So this specific image is using the default MongoDB port number,
which is 27017. And of course, it still, you know, populates a database which uses the same
name superdata. But talking about the credentials, yes, it uses a different set of credentials.
So I need to replace this with different credentials, which is non-prod user.
And for the password, again, this is a sensitive value, you can make use of the CI/CD variables
to mask it and refer to it over here. But just for the time being, I'm just going to, you know,
hard code the password here as well. So I'm just going to say password. Okay, so this is how you
can define a service and your job container can make use of these variables and connect to the
service using, you know, the hostname and the credentials required. Okay, so just to show you
that it's making use of the service and these variables, I'll just comment these variables so
that, you know, the job cannot reference those variables. Okay, so the same set of commands,
or the steps, I would like to just copy and just paste them in the code coverage because both the
jobs need to connect to the same database for, you know, doing the unit testing and the code
coverage. Right. So that's how you define a service. So let me quickly commit it. I'll just
say configured service container, and I'll just commit the changes to the feature branch.
So a new pipeline should be triggered. So let's go and check out the pipeline.
Yep. So let's go here. So you have two jobs running. Okay, as you can see, both the jobs
have been completed. So let's go ahead and check out the logs. So within the unit testing job,
so you see a lot of logs over here. So let me just, you know, minimize all this.
Okay. And okay. So the first thing is, it's preparing the Docker machine executor. So the
first thing it's doing is it's using the Docker executor with image Node.js 17 alpine. And it's also
starting the service. Right. So since I defined a service, so first, it's going to start the service
container, it's going to pull the Docker image, and it's going to run the MongoDB service connector.
And it's going to wait for the services to be up and running. And then it's going to start
the job image. Right. So it's going to pull the NodeJS image and run the image.
So once it is up and running, you can see that in the step script step, it is going to simply
run the NPM test. And it's going to make use of the service container as the MongoDB to connect

So even in code coverage, if you see it did run the code coverage command. And at the top,

the jobs are still able to run successfully based on the service container and the variables which

special service that sets up this nested Docker environment within your pipeline job. And Docker
in Docker also allows your GitLab jobs to run Docker commands regardless of the runners configuration
or privileges. So this is particularly useful for tasks like you know, building Docker images within
your pipeline, you can directly you know, run the build and the push image commands using the Docker
CLI. And it's going to you know, build and push the images. Apart from that, the Docker in Docker
is also useful in testing applications that rely on Docker. Okay, so that's the reason you know,
we are using the service Docker in Docker. And apart from that, I just want to add another keyword.

that this job does not require any artifacts. So that can be defined using dependencies. So what
are dependencies? So if you go to the CI/CD YAML syntax reference, and search for dependencies,
it has its own documentation. So this keyword, you know, defines the list of specific jobs from where
the artifacts have to be downloaded or fetched. Or it can also be used to configure the job to not
download any artifacts at all. So if you don't want to download any artifacts, you simply have to
you know, give an empty array, like, you know, I've just defined it over here. Okay, so that's how

============================================================
  50-Understanding_Deployment_Pipeline
============================================================

Let's have a brief overview about Kubernetes.
Kubernetes is an open-source container orchestration platform, which was originally developed by Google and now is maintained by the CNCF.
It is designed to automate the deployment, scaling, and management of containerized applications.
The first major component of Kubernetes is a node.
Nodes are the individual machines, physical or virtual, that make up a Kubernetes cluster.
Nodes can be categorized either as worker nodes or control nodes.
Worker nodes run all the containers, while the controller nodes manage the overall cluster.
The controller node includes the API server, controller manager, scheduler, and etcd.
The API server is the entry point for managing the cluster and serves the Kubernetes API.
A pod is the smallest deployable unit in Kubernetes. It represents a single instance of a running process in the cluster.
Pods can contain one or more containers that share the same network namespace, storage, and IP address.
They are often used to group containers that need to work together.
In our example, we only have one container in a pod.
If a pod experiences an issue, it is removed and it does not automatically restart.
To prevent the need of manual intervention, we can utilize replication controllers or deployments which handle the task of pod recovery.
Deployments are a high-level abstraction for managing replica sets and pods.
They provide declarative updates to applications, allowing you to describe an application's desired state,
and Kubernetes will handle the details of updating the actual state to match the desired state.
Once the pods are up and running, how do we access them?
We can access them using services.
Services in Kubernetes provide a stable and consistent network endpoint to access one or more pods.
They can load balance traffic among multiple pods, allowing applications to be easily scaled without affecting external clients.
Kubernetes offers several types of services to enable network communication between pods.
One of these types is load balancer service, which is used to expose your service to the external world, typically in a cloud environment.
It's going to create an external load balancer like AWS ELB or GCP load balancer, which is going to route the traffic to your service.
It's important to note that the availability and features of load balancer services may vary depending on the cloud provider or the on-premises infrastructure you are using.
Additionally, using load balancer services may incur additional costs, particularly when considering that a distinct load balancer may be required for each individual pod or application you wish to expose.
So to avoid using multiple load balancers, one can make use of Ingress as an alternative solution.
Kubernetes Ingress is designed to manage and route HTTP and HTTPS traffic into the cluster.
It provides more advanced and flexible traffic routing capabilities than a load balancer service.
It is suitable for exposing multiple services under a single domain or for setting up more complex routing rules.
You can route traffic based on paths, domain names, and other HTTP request attributes.
So when services are exposed through Ingress, they're often configured with a service type of ClusterIP for several reasons.
One major reason is cluster IP services provide internal access to the pods within the cluster but are not directly exposed externally.
This allows you to have fine-grained control over which services are accessible within the cluster, ensuring that not every service is exposed by default.
However, it's also important to note that the choice of service types and Ingress configuration depends on your specific use case and requirements.
Thank you.

============================================================

the next session, we are going to set up a couple of integration tests so that, you know, we can,
really test if the application is up and running within the pipeline itself.

============================================================
  57-Job_-_Fetch_Ingress_URL_&amp;_Integration_testing
============================================================

Let's understand GitLab environments.
In software, environments are used to isolate different stages of the development process such as development, testing, and production.
This allows developers to work on new features without affecting existing users, and it allows testers to verify that new features work correctly before they are deployed to production.
Each environment has its own services such as databases, vault, REST APIs, etc.
Most of the services are secured using username password credentials or some use API keys and this varies for each environment.
In GitLab CI/CD, environments are a powerful feature that can help you to organize, protect secrets, and also to visualize your deployments.
Here we need to discuss two terms, environments and deployments.
So what are deployments?
Each time GitLab CI/CD deploys a version of code to an environment, a deployment is created.
GitLab provides a full history of the deployments to each environment where you can track the history of the deployments to each environment making it easy to understand what's currently deployed and you also have the option to redeploy and also to rollback if necessary.
By the way, rollback is a feature which can revert to a previous deployment in case of issues and it's going to preserve the stability and minimize downtime.
That's about deployments.
Coming back to environments, you can think of environments as labels.
They represent stages where your code gets deployed such as development, staging, or production.
They don't hold the actual code, they just manage the deployment process.
Moreover, they act as reference points where the CI/CD pipeline targets specific environments for deployment and GitLab is going to track the history of all these deployments.
There are two main types of GitLab environments, static and dynamic.
Static environments are predefined and configured manually within the GitLab UI or using the GitLab CI/CD YAML file.
These are ideal for simple deployments with fixed configurations.
Whereas dynamic environments are created automatically for each pipeline run with their own unique URLs and names based on the CI/CD variables.
They are useful for situations with numerous deployments or where environment configurations vary significantly.
Apart from the static and dynamic environments, we also have additional environment types such as review apps and protected environments.
Review apps are temporary environments created from Merge Requests for reviewers to test changes before merging.
And whereas protected environments require additional approvals before deployments, it's going to add an extra layer of security for critical deployment purposes.
So talking about secrets or variables.
So by default, every job within a pipeline has access to all CI/CD variables.
So if a test job within a pipeline runs a compromised tool, it has the potential to expose all the CI/CD variables utilized by a deployment job.
This situation is often encountered in supply chain attacks and GitLab addresses this concern by restricting the environment scope of a variable.
One of the powerful features of GitLab CI/CD environments is the ability to control the scope of the CI/CD variable.
This adds a layer of security and flexibility to your deployments by ensuring sensitive information is only accessible when needed.
So here's a detailed breakdown of the environment scope.
So the environment scope basically allows you to restrict the specific CI/CD variables to one or a limited set of environments.
So there are several compelling reasons to use the environment scopes.
The first one is security.
By limiting access to sensitive information like database credentials, API keys, or production passwords to only the environment where they are needed, this minimizes the risk of accidental exposure if other environments are compromised.
The second one is about configuration management.
So different environments might require different configurations or settings.
So by scoping variables to a specific environment, you can ensure only the relevant configurations are applied and avoid conflicts or errors.
And the last one is improved pipeline efficiency.
By scoping variables, it reduces the overall footprint of your pipelines, making them lighter and faster to run.
It also prevents unnecessary exposure of sensitive information in logs or outputs of jobs that wouldn't need them.
So in this provided example, we have two variables with the same key named as kube_namespace.
But if you see, the values differ based on the environment scope.
Similarly, the MongoDB underscore password key value can only be accessed by jobs which are configured to deploy to the production environment.
So that's the basics about environments and the deployments.
Thank you.

============================================================
  59-Environment_and_Deployments
============================================================

In this session, we will understand what, why, and how about GitLab Kubernetes Agent.
In our previous demo, we deployed an application to Kubernetes by establishing a connection to
the cluster through the Kubeconfig file which is stored as a variable. So your Kubeconfig file
contains sensitive information like the URL of the Kubernetes API server, authentication
credentials, and the default namespace you use. If this information falls into the wrong hands,
they could gain unauthorized access to your Kubernetes cluster and can perform malicious
actions such as deploying malware, deleting resources, or even stealing data. So despite
storing the Kubeconfig file as a masked CI/CD variable within your GitLab server, it still
poses a security concern. Beyond security considerations, there is also a potential
for increased complexity when establishing a connection to the Kubernetes cluster,
especially if the cluster is situated behind a firewall or a network address translation,
which is NAT. So to address these issues, let's explore what the GitLab Agent for Kubernetes
brings to the picture. So the GitLab Kubernetes Agent is a key element within GitLab's
suite of tools designed to facilitate GitOps style deployments. Yes, you heard it right.
So GitLab facilitates GitOps through its integration with Flux. Note that this course
will not explore GitLab's GitOps capabilities, but with GitOps, you can also manage containerized
clusters and applications from a GitLab repository that acts as a single source of truth for your
system. While GitLab Agent's primary role involves GitOps implementation, its functionality extends
far beyond this particular use case. It provides a means of secure communication between GitLab
and the Kubernetes clusters without the need for exposing the Kubernetes API
and storing the credentials in third-party systems. The agent is implemented in a client
server configuration. The Kubernetes cluster side component is called Agent K and the GitLab
server side component is called KAS, which is short for GitLab Agent Server. Communication
between these agent components is initiated by Agent K because the connection must be initiated
from inside the Kubernetes cluster to bypass any firewall or NAT that the cluster may be located
behind. As the channel is bidirectional in nature, in the context of GitOps, this enables either push
or pull-based operations. So here, the GitLab server is the main application which talks to KAS.
So AgentK is the GitLab agent. It's going to keep a connection established to the KAS
instance, waiting for a request to process. It may also actively send information about things
which are happening within the cluster. But what about KAS? KAS is responsible for accepting
requests from the AgentK, authentication of requests from AgentK by querying GitLab.
After that, it's also going to fetch the agent's configuration from a corresponding Git repository
by querying Gitly. So what is Gitly? So Gitly provides high-level RPC access to Git repositories
and it is used by GitLab to read and write Git data. This is the method through which the AgentK
establishes communication, forwarding requests, responses, sending notifications, and also handles
other interactions between them. GitLab server takes care of managing the KAS
and it's going to eliminate the need for users to upgrade AgentK within their clusters whenever
there are updates or additional features. And GitLab also prefers implementing modifications
and logic directly to KAS instead of the AgentK. And given the lightweight nature of AgentK,
GitLab suggests having at least one instance of AgentK per cluster.
So how do we register this? So registering a GitLab Kubernetes Agent, you know, it's going to
connect with your Kubernetes cluster to GitLab. So what are the prerequisites? So we need to have
a GitLab account with sufficient permissions to create and manage projects and the Kubernetes
cluster. Whereas the GitLab project is where we want to, you know, register the agent and this
project will host the agent configuration files and it's also going to manage the deployments.
And finally, we also need a Kubernetes cluster which meets the minimum version requirement for
the GitLab agent compatibility. So to register, we have to navigate to the GitLab project,
click on the Operate and then choose Kubernetes cluster. We can create a new agent by specifying
a unique name. GitLab is going to automatically generate a unique and secure token for the agent
and it's also going to, you know, give you the Helm installation commands. The Helm chart
uses the generated token and the KAS address to enable the registration and the communication
process. And by running the Helm chart commands on your Kubernetes cluster, we can complete the
installation phase. After successful installation, the agent connects to the GitLab project or the
server and becomes visible in the Kubernetes cluster section of your project. We can further
manage the GitLab agent in your project using a config file located at the .gitlab
slash agent slash the agent name slash config.yaml file within your project repository.
This file is going to allow you to specify GitHub settings which define how the agent monitors
changes and triggers deployments for automated workflows. It will also help you to set CI/CD
access control to limit which projects and users can use the agent for deployments. And finally,
you can also configure remote development by defining settings
for creating and managing workspaces within your clusters. Thank you.

============================================================
  60-Connecting_a_Kubernetes_cluster_with_GitLab_via_agentK
============================================================

Let's do a quick recap of GitLab Agent and then we'll install it within our Kubernetes cluster.
So as discussed earlier, the GitLab integration with Kubernetes is going to help you to install,
configure, manage, deploy, and troubleshoot your cluster applications. And with the GitLab Agent,
you can connect clusters even if they are running behind a firewall and have real-time access to the
API endpoints. So it has complete documentation about how to connect your Kubernetes clusters
with GitLab Agent. And if you want to use GitOps with FlexCD, it also has a separate
documentation for that and how to use it in the GitLab CI/CD as well. Okay. So let's go and
configure it within our project. So if I go to my project, I can go to operate. In operate, I can go

  63-Manually_Promote_to_Staging_Environment_via_agentK

Let us understand how we can optimize our GitLab CI/CD configuration.
When creating an application in any programming language or setting up an automated pipeline
through a CI tool, you have likely encountered situations where you end up copying and
pasting portions of your code or pipeline from previous work. While copying CI pipelines can
accelerate initial project setup, relying heavily on duplication can hinder effective
maintenance and scalability. So we should always consider modularizing your CI pipelines to avoid
copy-pasting and facilitate easier updates and adjustments as the pipeline evolves.
GitLab CI/CD has multiple features which help you to get rid of the duplicated code
and to standardize your workflows which will help you to boost the development speed
and also it makes implementing a pipeline a simple process.
So to optimize your GitLab CI/CD pipelines, you can use other features such as the
Extends keyword, Anchor, Reference tags, Templates, and CI/CD components.
In this video, we will talk about the first three topics,
but before that you need to know about a concept called hidden jobs.
GitLab CI/CD has a concept of a hidden job. Any job which starts with a dot are not run by
GitLab CI/CD pipeline but can be used for disabling a job without deleting it or for reusable
configuration. These reusable templates and configuration blocks improve your pipeline's
modularity and maintainability. So let's understand how the extend keyword works.
So let's take an example and understand how the keyword is used. So imagine we have a pipeline
with two NodeJS jobs for unit testing and code coverage. So if you see this in a pipeline code,
it shares a lot of setup like using the same container image, service containers,
caching strategy, and also a before script for the dependency installation.
Although this example involves only two jobs, in other scenarios there might be many more jobs
using a common set of configuration. So how do we modularize them? So the Extends keyword
in GitLab CI/CD offers a solution. It's going to create a hidden job with the shared configuration
and we can let other jobs inherit it. So here's how it works. So the first step is to create a
hidden job with any name. In this example, we have named it as .base NodeJS job which contains
the common configuration such as the image, services, cache, and the before script. The
second step is to inherit the hidden job. So in the unit testing and the code coverage jobs,
we use the Extends keyword to specify the .base NodeJS job as the parent.
Once it is done, the GitLab CI/CD merges the parent configuration with the child's job,
overriding any duplicate values from the parent. And for your reference, here the line numbers
from 3 to 12 will be merged in both the unit testing and the code coverage jobs.
So this approach offers several benefits such as you know it's going to eliminate
repetitive setup code making pipelines easier to maintain, it's going to separate the configuration
concerns for better clarity, and any changes to the shared configuration are going to be applied
to all the inheriting jobs automatically. And finally, we can also build reusable templates
for common patterns streamlining pipeline creation.
And the next one we are going to talk about anchors. So if you're looking for ways to
further optimize your pipeline setup, we should not miss out on the anchors. So they are like
extents, mini-me helping you reuse smaller configurations for maximum efficiency.
So YAML specification generally has a feature called anchors that you know lets you use
the duplicate content across your YAML documents. Since GitLab CI/CD is written in YAML format,
we can use anchors with hidden jobs to share configurations between jobs.
So to use a hidden job as an anchor, you can define an anchor using the &amp; symbol or the &amp; symbol
followed by a name. In this example, we named it as node-config-anchor and this has to be provided
at the hidden job level. And to use the anchor and merge the configuration into any job or a
current job, you need to define a couple of left arrow symbols and call it by its name
with the hashtag symbol. So both of these can be seen on lines 14 and 19 in this slide.
And that's it. After these changes, the hidden job will be used by both the unit testing
as well as the code coverage jobs. So you might be asking like, so what is the difference? Like
why do we need to use extends and anchors? Because both of them are doing the same job,
you know, they are, you know, extending or they are, you know, merging the parent
configuration with the child jobs. So when comparing the extends keyword to anchors,
it is important to note that YAML anchors cannot be used across multiple files.
Anchors are only applicable within the same file where they are defined. So for the purpose of
reusing configurations from different YAML files, the recommended approach is to utilize
either the extends keyword or there is a keyword called reference tags. So since we have
discussed the extends and the anchors keyword, let's dive into the concept of reference tags.
Using the reference custom YAML tag, you know, to select keyword configurations from other jobs
sections and we can reuse it in the current section. Unlike the YAML anchors, you can use
the reference tag to reuse configuration from different YAML files. Imagine a pipeline with
two NodeJS jobs like we have seen earlier, the unit testing and the code coverage. So they share
a couple of common keywords like, you know, images, before script and a few things else.
So to achieve optimization, we can also create a hidden job with the commonly used configuration.
So we create a hidden job named as .base NodeJS config containing the shared configuration
like cache strategy, rules and the script command for test and code coverage jobs.
So now let's explore how we can refer to this configuration from the hidden job within the
unit testing job. So for the images and the before script keyword, we are directly specifying
the values as you can see here. But however, for the cache and the script keywords, we are utilizing
the reference keyword along with the hidden job name and tag. And now moving on to the code coverage
job, the image and before script keywords can be directly referenced from the unit testing job.
This highlights the versatility of reference tags, enabling the referencing of keywords
from both hidden and regular jobs. For the remaining script and the rules keyword,
the configuration is referred from the .base NodeJS config hidden job.
So upon implementing these steps, the final merged or expanded configuration of the pipeline
takes the following form. And if you notice, the rules keyword is only used in the Code Coverage
job and not in the Unit Testing job as it was only referenced in the Code Coverage job. Thank you.

Let's go ahead and see how the anchor keyword can be used for reusing the configuration.
So, if we come back to the same optimized GitLab CI/CD configuration documentation,
we also have anchors. So, this is a feature from YAML specification which can be used to
duplicate content across your YAML documentation. So, you can use anchors to duplicate or inherit
properties. And within GitLab, you can use anchors with hidden jobs to provide templates
for other jobs which are referencing it. So, how do you use this anchors keyword?
So, if you take this example, anchor can be added for a complete job and it can be referred to in other
jobs. So, you can use the ampersand symbol to create or define an anchor and you can
give it a unique name. So, this is given at the hidden job level. And once you add an anchor,
you can refer to it at any job by adding the contents of the alias. So, you can use this less than
symbol. And in order to include the anchor, you need to use hash string. So, this double less
than symbol is basically used for merging the given hash into this specific job and to include
the name of the anchor, you need to use the hash string. So, as I have told you,
these anchors can be added at the complete hidden job level or you can also refer to individual keywords
as well. So, if you want to refer to individual keywords, if you see this example, in the first
hidden job, you are defining an anchor at the job name level and at the second hidden job,
you are defining an anchor at the service level. So, in this way, you can refer to both
the job template as well as the service in a single job. So, if you take this example,
the test Postgres, it is going to refer to the complete job configuration using this double
less than equals symbol and hash string the job alias. It is going to refer to the complete
configuration defined under the job template. And then for the services, you can only use the
hash string and the alias which you provided here. So, this is going to refer to the service
configuration from this hidden job. So, these are the couple of different options you have.

Let's talk about templates and how you can use them within your GitLab CI/CD configuration.
While GitLab CI provides powerful tools for defining your CI/CD pipelines,
manually writing YAML code for every project can be tedious and prone to error. This is where
templates come into the picture. It offers several compelling advantages that enhance your DevOps
workflow. GitLab CI templates are pre-configured YAML files that you can include in your GitLab
CI YAML file to define your pipeline stages and jobs. GitLab already has numerous
pre-made templates for different programming languages, security scanning, and others. So
these templates basically come in two main types. So you have the pipeline templates and the job
templates. Pipeline templates define an entire CI/CD pipeline for a specific project type
or language like NodeJS application or a Ruby on Rails project. And whereas the job templates
define individual pipeline jobs such as a unit testing job, a security job like container scanning,
or a Docker image build job. So by using templates, you can save a lot of time and effort
by not writing the pipeline YAML code for your pipeline from scratch. And you can also easily
reuse templates across different projects. So consider there is a pre-built template tailored
for the NodeJS project that streamlines testing, building, and deployment process.
So it may include stages for unit testing to ensure code quality, code coverage to measure
how thoroughly the tests cover the code, building and pushing Docker images to Docker Hub for easy
sharing and deployment, and finally deploying to Amazon EKS, which is Elastic Kubernetes Service
for scalable container orchestration. So let's understand how this template can be used.
So let's assume we have team A, which is working on a project A. So it has adopted the NodeJS
template. It integrated it into their CI/CD GitLab CI configuration. And they have also adjusted some
test configurations and added credentials for the Docker Hub and the AWS S3. And hence,
they have automated the workflow. So they use the pipeline to efficiently test, build,
and deploy the application to AWS EKS. So here, the team A did not reinvent the wheel by writing
the GitLab CI configuration from scratch. They used an existing template, and they made some
small configurational changes like adding the credentials for their own Docker Hub accounts or
for connecting to their own AWS EKS cluster. And they just used the existing pipeline template.
So this is the way the templates help the teams to efficiently reuse existing pipeline configuration.
So let's take the same template and assume there is a team B working on project B,
and they also want to adopt the same NodeJS template. Let's assume they reuse the template's
core components by leveraging the unit testing and the code coverage stages, but
they have a different containerization and deployment strategy. So what they can do is,
they can modify the other stages and tailor them to push the images to Google Container Registry
and deploy to the Google Kubernetes Engine (GKE) instead of the EKS. So the existing template
can be used as building blocks. So you can use a couple of blocks which are existing,
and you can add your own stages as per your requirement. So what are the key takeaways?
So reusable templates promote efficiency. They save time and effort by providing a pre-built
structure for common tasks. Teams can customize and adapt templates to meet their specific needs
and infrastructure choices. And modular design enables selective reuse. Teams can pick and
choose the stages they need, making the template adaptable to various project requirements.
So how do we store the templates? Where do we access them? So templates may be stored
in various locations including local repositories, private repositories,
GitLab provided templates, and templates from other projects. So how can we reference and
include these templates in our pipelines? So to include an external YAML file into your
CI/CD configuration, we can utilize the includes keyword. So let's take an example. So let's assume
you have a job template within the same project directory as the .gitlab-ci.yml file.
So we can make use of the include local keyword to include a file from the same repository and
branch. We can also use a shorter syntax to specify the path without the local keyword as well.
And for job templates which are hosted in a private GitLab instance project, we can use
the include remote keyword to include the YAML files from another GitLab instance. And similar
to the local keyword, a shorter syntax without the remote keyword can also be used here as well.
However, be careful when you are including another project's CI/CD configuration file
because from a security perspective, this is similar to pulling a third-party dependency.
And if the templates are in another private project of the same GitLab instance,
we can use the include project keyword. And the possible inputs for this type are,
you know, you can include the project name which contains the full GitLab project path,
you can include a file path or an array of file paths relative to the root directory.
The YAML file must have the .yml or .yaml extension. And you can also add an optional
include ref which specifies the ref to retrieve the file from. And if you don't mention this,
it always defaults to the head of the project when not specified.
And one last thing is if you wish to use the GitLab's pre-built templates such as the code
quality template or security scanning templates which are generally stored within the GitLab
official repository under the lib/GitLab/CI/templates directory, you can utilize
the include template keyword to include it within your GitLab CI configs.
Additionally, you can even control when a template is included using rules. For example,
with rules and if conditions, we can include the code quality template only when the commit branch
name starts with a key or a value as feature. Thank you.

Let's go ahead and configure container scanning within our pipeline. So again, we have a very
detailed documentation on what is container scanning and how to enable this and make
changes as required. So basically, this container scanning comes under the software composition
analysis, which is an aspect of inspecting the items your code uses. So these items typically include
application and system dependencies that are always imported from external sources.
So what GitLab offers is, it offers both container scanning and dependency scanning to ensure
coverage of all these dependency types. So in this demo, we'll only cover the container scanning
because that's available in the free tier. The dependency scanning is available only in the
ultimate tier. So we can't really focus much on that. And talking about the container scanning,
since we are creating Docker images, right? So you can, you know, test those Docker images
and to know the vulnerabilities. So basically, when you're creating a Docker image,
you also rely on a base image, right? So using the container scanning, you can also get to know
what are the existing vulnerabilities in the base image. And you can take a call based on,
you know, the report generated by this job. And internally, it's going to make use of the
vulnerability static analysis. Basically, it uses Trivy and GRI. So the default analyzer is Trivy.

Let's see how to integrate GitLab with your Slack workspace.
So, GitLab has various capabilities which can integrate with different third-party products,
and one of them is the Slack workspace.
So, it has a GitLab for Slack app, which can be used for sending the notifications
or also running your GitLab CI/CD jobs using some commands in your Slack workspace.
So, how do we integrate this?
Again, there is complete documentation available here, but I'll be showing you a quick demo.
So, for this, you need to have a Slack workspace.
I'm using a free workspace over here, a trial version, and I have various channels.
So, let me create a new channel for this demo.
So, let me name this channel as GitLab Notifications.
So, let me also make this a private channel so that only specific people can check this out.
And you can skip adding people.
So, once the channel is created, you need to also integrate your Slack workspace with GitLab.
So, you can quickly add that integration.
So, once it is added, you can invite GitLab.
So, there is a GitLab application available.

Let's have a look at what is GitLab ChatOps and how you can use this with the Slack workspace.
So you can use GitLab ChatOps to interact with CI/CD jobs through your chat services like Slack.
And the reason is, many organizations use Slack or Mattermost to collaborate, troubleshoot,
and plan the work. And with ChatOps, you can discuss work with your team,
run CI/CD jobs, and also view the job output all from the same application.
So what are the requirements for this? The first requirement is, of course, you need to
have the GitLab for Slack app integrated within your project. And then you can make use of the
Slack slash commands. If you're using Mattermost, it has a different set of commands.

deploying from one environment to another environment, and also for running the jobs.

  75-Pipeline_Monitoring_with_Prometheus_and_Grafana

Let's talk about how to add project batches.
So batches are a unified way to present condensed information about your projects.
So a batch consists of a small image and a URL that the image points to.
So in GitLab, batches are displayed below the project description,
and they can be displayed both at the project and at the group level.
So how do you create these batches?
So you have three types of batches, pipeline status batch, test coverage report batch,
and the latest release batch, but it also supports some custom batches.
So if you're interested, you can just go and check them out.
So there are various different statuses for pipeline batches.
And if it is a code coverage report batch, it gives you the percentage of
code covered based on the code coverage job.
You can also customize the batches by having different styles,
text, and images as well based on your requirement.
So let's see how to, you know, define or create these batches for our projects.

============================================================
  76-Adding_a_Pipeline_status_&amp;_Code_Coverage_Badge
============================================================

Let's talk about self-managed runners.
In our earlier sessions, we discussed runners, which are essentially virtual machines responsible for performing various tasks within a GitLab CI/CD pipeline.
For example, a runner can handle tasks such as cloning your repository, installing necessary software, and executing specific commands.
We have also discussed about the SAST runners, which are hosted and managed by GitLab.
These runners are fully integrated with GitLab.com and are enabled by default for all projects with no configuration required.
Each GitLab-hosted runner is essentially a fresh virtual machine that GitLab hosts for you.
So you have the options to choose from different operating systems like Linux, Windows, MacOS, and GPU-enabled SAST runners.
So what are self-managed runners?
Self-managed runners are machines that you deploy and manage yourself.
This gives you more control over the hardware, operating system, and the software tools that your workflow runs on.
Self-managed runners can be run on any operating system, including custom OS as well.
So here are some reasons why you might choose to use the self-hosted runners or the self-managed runners.
So the first one is about customizable environment.
You can set up your Runner with the specific software and resources needed for your projects, giving you complete control over the execution environment.
This can be useful for running jobs that require specific tools or libraries or that cannot be run on shared runners due to security restrictions.
In some organizations, strict security and compliance policies may prevent the use of GitLab-hosted runners for certain workflows.
So self-managed runners can be set up in a controlled environment, preventing conflicts and potential security vulnerabilities that could arise from sharing resources with other projects.
Depending on the demand for GitLab-hosted runners in your organization, there might be wait times for available runners.
So self-managed runners eliminate this wait time as they are dedicated to your projects.
You can scale your self-managed runner pool based on your project needs.
This can be particularly useful if you have many concurrent workflows or if you need to run multiple workflows in parallel.
Finally, self-managed runners can be placed in specific geographic locations to reduce latency for your CI/CD workflows, especially if you have a global user base or need to comply with data residency regulations.
In summary, while GitLab-hosted runners are convenient and suitable for many use cases, self-managed runners provide more control, flexibility, and customization options.
So the next step is to understand how to configure the runners in the context of GitLab CI/CD.
GitLab runners or the GitLab self-managed runners can be of three types.
So you can add them as shared runners which are available to all groups and projects within a GitLab instance.
This could be group runners which are available to all projects and subgroups in a group.
And finally, at the project level, so you can also add project runners which are associated with specific projects.
Typically, project runners are used by one project at a time, but we can also overwrite this configuration as well.
To set up a self-managed runner as a project runner, you can navigate to the desired project or repository's CI/CD settings.
There you will find an option to disable the shared runner for the project and also to create new project-level runners under the Runners section.
During the setup, you will have the opportunity to select the operating system of the machine where you will install the runner, add tags, and configure additional settings.
You can use tags to control the jobs a runner can run.
You can also check the Run Untagged Jobs box if you don't want to restrict this runner to specific jobs based on tags.
You can click Create Runner to generate a registration token to register the runner with GitLab.
Along with the token, the GitLab UI also provides steps to install the GitLab Runner application for the selected environments and architectures.
When setting up a runner for the first time on an operating system, it is essential to install the GitLab Runner application prior to registering the runner.
So, what are the steps required?
We first download the binary for your system using the curl command.
We give it permissions to execute using chmod.
We create a GitLab Runner user and also select a home directory.
And finally, we install and run the Runner as a service.
Now that the GitLab Runner application is installed, the next step is to register the runner.
During the registration process, you will be prompted to provide some details.
So, after you enter the GitLab Runner register command, the first thing it's going to ask for is your GitLab instance URL.
So, this ensures that the runner connects to the correct GitLab instance.
And then it's going to ask you for a name.
So, this is the name of the runner which is going to help you easily identify it within your CI/CD infrastructure.
And then you can also select the type of the executor which you want to use for this runner.
This determines how jobs will be executed such as using a Shell Executor or a Docker Executor.
And upon successful registration, all these configuration settings are automatically saved into a file named as config.toml.
This file serves as a central reference for managing the runner's behavior and settings.
To further customize your runner, we can edit this config.toml file located within the GitLab Runner installation directory on the machine.
So, this file allows you to define the runner name, the tags for identification, executors like Docker or Shell Executor to determine how the job runs.
You can also define resources like CPU, memory, and disk limits.
You can add environment variables for job executions.
And you can also add caching paths and many other configurations.
Finally, if you want to check the status of the setup, you can again go back to your GitLab project level CI/CD settings and check the runner status.
And you can also use the tags at a job level to select a specific runner from the list of all runners that are available for the project.
Thank you.

  81-Use_Local_Template_to_Upload_Reports_to_AWSMinio_S3_Bucket
============================================================

Let's talk about GitLab Auto DevOps.
Auto DevOps is a feature within GitLab that takes CI/CD automation to the next level.
It essentially configures and runs a complete CI/CD pipeline for you,
eliminating the need for manual setup.
So, what can we expect from the Auto DevOps?
So, Auto DevOps is going to analyze your project's programming language and framework
to determine the appropriate tools and settings.
It sets up stages for building, testing,
and deploying your application using industry best practices.
It's also going to integrate vulnerability scanning,
code quality checks, container scanning,
dependency scanning, and other security measures into the Pipeline.
It can also create review apps for testing
the application before merging to the source branch.
And post-merging, we can also deploy the application
either using continuous delivery or continuous deployment.
So, overall, Auto DevOps is a very powerful tool,
but it's not a one-size-fits-all solution.
So, depending on your project complexity and preferences,
you may need to further customize the pipeline.
So, let's see how this works at the project level.
Once the Auto DevOps is enabled at the CI/CD settings of the project,
it starts by detecting your code language
and uses CI/CD templates to build and run default pipelines.
It starts with the build job.
Auto DevOps leverages a pre-existing Dockerfile, if present,
or applies Heroku buildpacks to construct a Docker image
by identifying the coding language.
Regardless of the path taken,
a Docker image will be generated by the end of the build job.
Next is the testing stage.
During the test stage of the GitLab Auto DevOps,
your code gets put through its paces
using both your project's existing tests
and the power of Heroku buildpacks.
These handy tools simplify application testing
for various languages,
taking some of the heavy lifting off your shoulders.
However, it is important to note that
not all programming languages are currently supported in this stage.
Few of the supported languages are Ruby, NodeJS,
Maven, Gradle, Python, Java, Go, and few others.
So, this stage also includes code quality analysis,
static application security testing,
auto secret detection, container scanning, and many more.
So, metrics and reports of all these jobs
will be available for you to analyze
at the end of the pipeline.
Auto DevOps can also be configured
to deploy applications to Kubernetes clusters.
So, we can configure our GitLab project
with Kubernetes clusters.
So, it supports different Kubernetes clusters.
Few of them are Amazon Elastic Container Service,
Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service,
Amazon EC2, Google Kubernetes Engine, and Bare Metal.
After the testing stage,
if deployment is configured,
the next stage is review apps.
The review stage is available during merge request.
So here, GitLab DevOps automatically deploys the changes
to a different environment
to review how the application works
before deploying to production.
It uses a Helm auto deploy chart
to deploy review applications.
This Helm chart can be modified as per the requirements.
After you deployed your code changes into a review app,
GitLab Auto DevOps takes things a step further.
It's not only just about seeing your application in action,
it's about ensuring it's secure
and ready for the real world.
That's where dynamic application security testing comes in.
So, think of DAST as a tester for your review apps.
It uses the open source OWASP ZAP Proxy tool
to scan your running applications,
searching high and low for any potential
security vulnerabilities or suspicious behavior.
And no need to worry about the manual setup,
Auto DevOps integrates DAST into the review apps workflow.
Once your app is deployed,
the ZAP Proxy gets to work mimicking real world attacks
and probing for any weaknesses.
If the ZAProxy uncovers any security vulnerabilities,
you'll get a detailed report
and this report pinpoints the vulnerabilities,
explains the severity,
and even offers suggestions for fixing them.
Depending on your Auto DevOps deployment configuration,
after merging the feature branch to the source branch,
Auto DevOps automatically deploys that
to the staging or production environment.
And when your application reaches production or staging,
Auto DevOps does not stop.
It runs the browser performance testing
to see how quickly your page loads.
This test compares your newer version to the older versions,
making sure it doesn't get sluggish over time.
But what about deployments?
Auto DevOps offers three different deployment options
for tailoring your application updates.
These are continuous deployment to production,
automatic staging, but manual production deployment,
and we also have something called
continuous deployment to production
using timed incremental rollouts.
So the first option,
which is the continuous deployment to production,
here, the updates are directly pushed
to the production environment whenever a code change.
So there is no manual intervention in the first option.
Well, talking about the second option,
updates are automatically flown to the staging environment.
It's going to wait for a manual approval
before hitting the production.
Optionally, GitLab features allow staged rollouts
in a controlled, incremental,
percentage-based fashion as well.
For example, if you have 10 pods and a 10% rollout,
one pod updates with the newer version
while others remain unchanged.
So manual release of each rollout stage
is triggered by clicking a play button.
The third option is the timed rollout option,
similar to the manual staging,
but each rollout stage automatically deploys
after a set delay, ideally in minutes.
So it is, again, customizable.
So you can customize the delay times
for added control over update progression.
And for example, this, the default setting,
the continuous deployment with five-minute intervals
between each rollout stage.
So by choosing this approach,
I don't think, you need to choose the approach
that best suits your needs
and you can ensure seamless and controlled updates
for your applications.
Thank you.

Let's go ahead and do the canary deployment using the GitLab Auto DevOps features.
So back in the GitLab documentation, you can also use a deployment strategy where you can automatically deploy to a staging environment and manually deploy to production.
So we are making use of the third strategy.
So here, the pipeline is going to, you know, continuously deploy to the Staging Environment in the default branch.
And after staging deployment is successful, it's going to continuously deliver to the production.
So if you see the terms here in staging, it's going to do continuous deployment where it's going to automatically deploy the application.
And whereas to production, it is continuous delivery where you have to manually, you know, deploy the application to the production.
So talking about the setup, it's going to use a CI/CD variable called as staging enabled to one.
It's going to enable the staging environment and it's going to use incremental rollout mode to manual.
Okay, so what is this incremental rollout mode?
So what is canary?
So canary deployments are a popular continuous deployment strategy where, you know, you want, I mean, this is used where you want to, you know,
ship certain features to only a portion of your pods and watch their behavior as you increase the percentage of your user base who is visiting the deployed feature.
And if all works well, you can deploy the feature to production knowing that it shouldn't cause any problems.
And in the other case, if there are any issues while, you know, you are deploying to the smaller fleet of pods, you can quickly roll back as well.
Right. So that's how the canary deployment works.
And if you want to see how this works in GitLab CI, you can click on this incremental rollout.
It's going to, you know, simply explain like, you know, it's going to do a rollout manually.
So you can, you know, increment the, you know, the number of pods by 10 percent, 25, 50 and up to 100 percent.
So, for example, if you have like the number of replicas of pods, if you have 10 and if you try to roll out 10 percent, it's going to deploy the newer version of the application to a single pod.
And the remaining nine pods will be of the older version.
So you can, you know, increase the number of newer version pods by rolling out 25 percent and 50 percent.
And if anything is not working as expected, you can again, you know, roll back to 10 percent also.
And once you roll out 100 percent, there is no coming back.
So you can't scale down. So you must do a complete rollback of your deployment.
So let's try to understand this, you know, through a demo.
So back in my, you know, Solar System Auto DevOps project, the first thing which I would like to do is I would like to go to the CI/CD settings.

Let's go ahead and see what is this rollback deployment option and how it can be used in our
example. So if I go back, generally rollbacks are used in order to undo any deployments if
there are any performance issues. But in our case, we don't have any performance issues,
so the application is working fine. But let's assume we have a lot of user complaints saying
that they don't like the new UI because everything is static, the background is static,
and the solar system is also static. It's not spinning anymore. So let's assume you're receiving
a lot of user bad reviews. So as a team, you would like to quickly rollback to the previously
working deployment. So how do we do that? So back in the documentation for environments
and deployments, there is a section called environment rollback. So you can rollback
your deployment to a specific commit. And when you do this, a new deployment is created,
and it's going to have its own unique job ID. And talking about rollbacks, if your
deployment job within your pipeline depends on the previously generated artifacts,
we must manually regenerate them in order to use those artifacts in a deploy job. So the rollback
is only going to trigger a deployment job, it won't trigger the other jobs. And for rolling back,
you can quickly go back to your environments. So this is the previous pipeline which we executed,
where we rolled out to 100% in production, and the output was this static page.
So to rollback to the previously running deployment, you can go back to your
operational environments. Since we want to do this in the production environment,
you have to go to production. And it's going to show you all the deployments which happened.
So currently, you know, I would like to, you know, rollback to my previous commit, which was,
you know, successful earlier. And you can only rollback to successful deployments, you can't
rollback to failed, you know, deployments. Okay, so if you see, I have an option called as rollback
environment. And the other option is, you can also redeploy the same, you know, commit to the
same environment as well. Okay, so you have these two options, redeploying and rolling back. So as
of now, I would like to rollback. Okay, so this is the commit to which I would like to rollback,
which worked fine. So let me click on, you know, rollback environment, it's going to, you know,

you know, environment. So before I approve this, so if I go to the job, so let me click on this
job ID, it's going to take me to the job. And this is going to run in the same pipeline. So if I go

So talking about the Auto DevOps deployment strategies, so far we explored the continuous
deployment to production and the automatic deployment to staging and manual deployment
to production strategies. So now let's explore the second one as well, which is continuous
deployment to production using timed incremental rollout. So again, this is a continuous deployment,
you don't have any manual option over here. So what is this about? So let's briefly understand this.
So this is going to use a timed incremental rollout to continuously deploy your
application. So it's going to start with 10% of your parts, then it's going to do a 25%
rollout, 50% followed by 100%. And the thing about this timed incremental is,
so each rollout is going to have a five-minute delay between the jobs. So every five minutes,
it's going to do the rollout and you have to keep on monitoring the application for any
issues and services. If you find any issues, you can simply stop the rollouts and roll back
quickly as seen earlier. So let's try to enable this and check this out.
